Queensland might not reopen to Australia until it reaches 90 per cent vaccination rate

Author: Gleeson, Peter

Publication info: The Courier - Mail (Online) ; Brisbane, Qld. [Brisbane, Qld]. 20 Sep 2021.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/queensland-might-not-reopen-australia-until/docview/2574568832/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=Gleeson%2C+Peter&amp;aulast=Gleeson&amp;issn=&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Queensland+might+not+reopen+to+Australia+until+it+reaches+90+per+cent+vaccination+rate&amp;jtitle=The+Courier+-+Mail+%28Online%29&amp;pubname=The+Courier+-+Mail+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Queensland+might+not+reopen+to+Australia+until+it+reaches+90+per+cent+vaccination+rate&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Despite NSW and Victoria outlining a road map to opening up based on an 80 per cent double vaccination rate, Queensland is yet to commit to any blueprint going forward.
The state’s population is on track to hit the 80 per cent double jab mark no later than December 5, giving hope for a more normal Christmas period.
But now some Labor insiders are claiming that a 90 per cent threshold is being weighed up.
Chief health officer Dr Jeannette Young last week publicly said that she would like to see 90 per cent of Queenslanders vaccinated.
Tasmania’s director of public health has publicly indicated he would like a 90 per cent vaccination rate in his state before its borders were fully opened the rest of the country.
Premier Annastacia Palaszczuk said it was too early to speculate on the 90 per cent target.
“We are waiting on updates – Doherty institute modelling – in two weeks before we can make those decisions,” she said
As well as Dr Young’s caution, politics is driving a shift in thinking to a higher threshold – with Labor polling finding Queenslanders do not want the borders if it means a mass Delta variant outbreak, like has happened in NSW and Victoria.
NED-4476-Covid-19-Vaccine-Rates-Brisbane
The polling makes two key findings. The first is that Queenslanders are angry at the so-called exemptions unit, which is regarded as “heartless’’, for refusing to allow Queenslanders back into the Sunshine State, but it gives special treatment to the wives and girlfriends of footballers.
The key finding, however, is that Queenslanders love their liberty and freedoms, and the fact that coronavirus is under control and that is a key driver in the Palaszczuk Government’s thinking.
Tourism industry leaders are incensed at the prospect of Queensland being closed for the Christmas-New Year holidays.

3000 may die on the road to freedom

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 20 Sep 2021: 4.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/3000-may-die-on-road-freedom/docview/2574093706/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=3000+may+die+on+the+road+to+freedom&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=3000+may+die+on+the+road+to+freedom&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=4&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Burnet Institute modelling underpinning the Andrews government’s road map for reopening Victoria predicts between 1455 and 3152 people will have died from Covid in the state between July 1 and December 31.
Funded by the state Health Department, institute authors Romesh Abeysuriya, Dominic Delport, Rachel Sacks-Davis, Margaret Hellard and Nick Scott considered four possible scenarios to estimate the number of infections, hospitalisations and ICU admissions.
These included a maintained lockdown, included as a counterfactual scenario to set baseline estimates from which restrictions are eased, and the road map as announced by the Andrews government on Sunday, which sees school and childcare gradually return throughout October, increased outdoor activities at 70 per cent full vaccination, and mandatory vaccination of authorised workers, teachers, childcare workers, parents of children in childcare, hospitality workers and hospitality patrons.
The two other scenarios considered included the road map with the added assumption vaccinated people continue to seek symptomatic testing at the same rate as non-vaccinated people and the road map with a 15 per cent reduction in non-household transmission.
Under the road map scenario cases are expected to peak at a median of 4543 a day (in a range of 2778 to 6761 cases) on a median date of December 15 (and between December 7 and December 22).
This would see demand for hospital beds peak at a median of 3150 (with a range of 1950 to 4400), and demand for ICU beds peak at a median 706 (with a range of 462 to 953). The median estimate for deaths between July 1 and December 31 — including 11 that have already occurred — is 2202, with a range of 1455 to 3152.
The Institute said the key question it set out to answer was: “As vaccine coverage increases, how best can restrictions be eased that prevent the health system capacity from being exceeded?” Its “Covasim” simulation model for the easing of restrictions over the October-December period takes in data on demographics, contact networks, workforce composition, contact tracing systems, and age-specific vaccination rates.
“As well as options for easing of restrictions, additional policies around vaccine allocation and testing were examined to determine potential approaches to further reduce the epidemic peak,” the modelling states.
The Burnett modellers argue even without any easing of restrictions, there is a “moderate risk” of exceeding health system capacity.
“Based on the current epidemic growth rate, a peak in seven-day average daily diagnoses of 1400-2900 is estimated to occur between 19-31 October,” the report states. “Corresponding peaks in hospital and ICU demand were 1200-2500 and 260-550 respectively, with 24 per cent of simulations resulting in hospital demand exceeding 2500 beds.” The modellers found that under the road map scenario, the significant easing of restrictions at 80 per cent vaccine coverage led to 63 per cent of simulations exceeding 2500 hospitalisations, and resulted in a second epidemic peak in mid-December.
However, they found high rates of symptomatic testing among vaccinated people could reduce the impact on the health system, reducing the 63 per cent chance of >2500 hospitalisations to 29 per cent, although they noted this “could be difficult to achieve in practice”. “If a 15 per cent reduction in non-household risk could be achieved and sustained through a variety of additional targeted public health and testing interventions, the risk of >2500 hospital demand could be reduced to 18 per cent,” they found.
“When 80 per cent adult vaccine coverage is reached, the case numbers, hospital and ICU numbers can provide a guide as to the likelihood of the health system capacity being exceeded and whether restrictions can be safely eased consistent with the road map or whether a more staggered approach may be required.
“Due to uncertainty about whether the epidemic growth rate will be sustained, seasonal impacts and vaccine efficacy parameters against the Delta strain, updated projections are required as more data becomes available.
“Decisions to ease restrictions should be based on the latest epidemiological and health system information.”
MELBOURNE ROAD MAP 70% DOUBLE DOSED OCTOBER 26 ► No restrictions on leaving home NN No curfew NN Travel limit increased to 25km.
► STUDENTS RETURN TO SCHOOL (VCE students return on October 6. Prep to Year 2 return on October 18) From October 26: Years 3 & 4 (Tues-Wed), Years 5 & 6 (Thurs-Fri), Year 7 (full-time, five days), Years 8 & 9 (Tues-Wed), Years 10 (Thurs-Fri) and Years 11 & 12 (full-time, five days) ► RETAIL general retail open for outdoor service only, plus click & collect ► HAIRDRESSING fully vaccinated: 5 people ► HOSPITALITY (pubs, restaurants, cafes): Food and drink seated service and outdoors only, fully vaccinated: 50 people ► ENTERTAINMENT VENUES fully vaccinated: 50 outdoors only ► PUBLIC GATHERINGS fully vaccinated: 10 people. Unvaccinated: 5 ► RELIGION Groups of 50 fully vaccinated or 20 unvaccinated people will be able to attend outdoor religious services ► FUNERALS fully vaccinated: 50 people. Unvaccinated: 20 – and only outdoors ► WEDDINGS Groups of 50 fully vaccinated or 10 unvaccinated people will be able to attend outdoor weddings ► MASKS will remain mandatory both indoors and outdoors

No limits on intrastate travel ► SCHOOLS all year levels return to class ► RETAIL All retail opens, with density caps ► PUBS, RESTAURANTS AND CAFES can seat up to 150 vaccinated guests indoors and 500 outdoors ► FOOD COURTS takeaway only ► HAIRDRESSERS fully vaccinated: 150 indoors ► MASKS only required indoors NN 10 visitors in homes and indoor community sport can resume ► Victorians can go to work if fully vaccinated ► Weddings, funerals and religious services can welcome 150 fully vaccinated people indoors and up to 500 outdoors

Dan’s slow road to nowhere

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 20 Sep 2021: 24.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/dan-s-slow-road-nowhere/docview/2574092354/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Dan%E2%80%99s+slow+road+to+nowhere&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Dan%26rsquo%3Bs+slow+road+to+nowhere&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=24&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: MELBOURNE’S gruelling lockdown will drag on for at least the next 36 days — three weeks longer than that expected in Sydney.
And even then, the October 26 ‘end to lockdown’ in greater Melbourne will mean something vastly different to the freedoms being returned to Sydneysiders, despite their vastly higher case numbers.
In reality, the Andrews government’s ‘reopening’ road map is a roadblock, keeping retail, much of hospitality and many levels of in-class education shut for almost a month longer than Sydney’s exit strategy.
Premier Daniel Andrews’s long-awaited detail on Victoria’s re-emergence from crushing Covid lockdown — with Melbourne to suffer the longest citywide closure in the world come early October — once again means more than five million people are stuck in desolate limbo. The Premier’s spin yesterday in setting forward dates for a complex array of minor changes to business settings and social reawakening may have been received by some, desperate for any relief, as at least a map of some sort.
But in the detail, the changes are glacial in comparison to not just Sydney but advanced nations such as Britain, the US and Israel that opened further and faster when vaccination rates were in the 60 per cent range.
In Andrews’ Victoria, Covid still controls every aspect of government intransigence and it’s pessimistic go-slow on reopening.
Already, across suburban Melbourne compliance with numbing restrictions is breaking and the Covid reproduction rates in some municipalities is more than twice the level seen in many Sydney municipalities. In other words, the virus is now spreading faster across parts of Melbourne than had ever been the case in Sydney because people, rightly or wrongly, have given up following the rules and have lost faith and patience in the Victorian government’s blunt lockdown response. But the Victorian government hasn’t matched the urgency and creativity of the NSW vaccine rollout, leaving us lagging behind.
While Mr Andrews yesterday underlined the Burnet Institute’s modelling that showed easing restrictions too quickly would have a significant effect on hospital capacities, he finally conceded broader consideration beyond just infection case numbers was required.
Even so, after 20 months of dealing with the pandemic, the only reason Victorian hospitals could be at risk of being overwhelmed now is a lack of appropriate government preparation and resourcing.
The Premier has attempted to present his road map as a plan for reopening, not a plan for remaining closed. Yet that is exactly what his strategy sets out. Almost two weeks ago, NSW Premier Gladys Berejiklian mapped out her state’s journey for reopening and it was the exact opposite of the Andrews doctrine in scope, pace and tone.
For those north of the Murray, the quicker route out of lockdowns and an economic deep freeze has been defined by hope and a determination to press forward with confidence.
The NSW map gives back freedoms at the earliest opportunity to those who took their responsibility to get vaccinated. In Victoria, while minor changes to outdoor sport and recreation from September 26 and, from October 26 some reopening for outdoor patrons in hospitality and pubs is also reserved for fully vaccinated people, they fall far short of what is being offered in NSW and should be delivered here.
Even the return for all Melbourne students across all levels to in-class learning from October 26 carried the heavy caveat “at least part time”.
For beleaguered greater Melbourne retailers — many of whom will not survive financially — shops will only re-open when Victoria hits 80 per cent double-dose adult vaccination on the forecast date of November 5. No other city in the world has imposed such draconian, stifling restrictions for so long under vaccination rates above 60, let alone 70 per cent.
And again, our neighbouring state will have seen most of these restrictions eased long before. In NSW, which expects to hit a 70 per cent fully vaccinated adult population on October 8, hospitality, retail, gyms and personal services can reopen with one person per 4sq m inside — a full month earlier than here.
From early October across Sydney, even racecourses, theme parks and zoos can reopen with the one person per 4sq m limit, while sports stadiums can have crowds of up to 5000 people with the same distancing requirements and up to 500 people can attend ticketed and seated outdoor events. Cinemas, theatres and galleries will also re-open in NSW at the 70 per cent fully-vaxxed milestone, with a limit of 75 per cent fixed seating capacity.
Australia’s two biggest cities may be less than 900km apart but there remains a universe of difference in the way citizens and businesses are being treated. That will be reflected in the respective time it takes for each state to recover from Covid and, at this crushing crawl, Victoria will feel the economic and societal pain for years longer. When Melbourne finally emerges from lockdown on October 26, we will have spent 267 days under effective house arrest. Mr Andrews will try to paint the occasion as a celebration. In truth, it will be a shameful indictment on a government that has failed the people of Melbourne and Victoria at almost every turn over the past 18 months.

Subject: Immunization

Even true libertarians can support vax passports

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 17 Sep 2021: 13.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/even-true-libertarians-can-support-vax-passports/docview/2573080477/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=unknown&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Even+true+libertarians+can+support+vax+passports&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Even+true+libertarians+can+support+vax+passports&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=13&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: It is undoubtedly true that preventing people who choose not to be vaccinated from engaging in ­activities that are open to the vaccinated involves a degree of coercion. But that hardly means any such restrictions ought to be opposed by those who place a high value on liberty.
There are, on the contrary, arguments for restrictions of this kind that should appeal even to libertarians.
After all, no one has a right to impose a harm on others by negligently disregarding their rights and interests. And it is surely clear that negligently infecting another person with a potentially serious disease breaches their right to live safely and securely, undermining a fundamental freedom that govern­ments have a duty to protect.
Indeed, for most accidental harms, the law of torts – which towering libertarians, such as Friedrich Hayek, have not only endorsed but eulogised – serves precisely that purpose, entitling the victim of a negligent act to rely on the coercive powers of the state to secure compensation from the act’s perpetrator.
Were tort law readily applicable to highly contagious diseases, it would not only help compensate victims; the threat of being sued would deter negligent transmission, inducing people to seek vaccination, or, knowing or suspecting that they have a communicable disease, to self-isolate.
There are, however, obvious difficulties in relying on the remedies offered by the law of tort to control the spread of Covid.
In part, those difficulties relate to the complexities of making out a case: it would, for example, be virtually impossible to unambiguously identify the culprits in a lengthy chain of transmission.
But even if those issues could be addressed, many of the costs of Covid do not fall on those who catch the disease; they are instead borne by society as a whole, through broader economic and social losses that tort law is neither designed to recover nor to effectively prevent.
And with an infection that can spread explosively, it is surely preferable to pre-emptively reduce the probability of outbreaks, rather than relying, almost certainly unsuccessfully, on private legal action to pursue those who helped trigger an outbreak once it had ­occurred.
Given those difficulties, other instruments – such as “vaccinal passports” – must fulfil the functions that tort law cannot serve, including preventing immediate harm and encouraging the taking of reasonable precautions.
Moreover, since those other instruments are merely regulatory substitutes for the (coercive) law of tort, it is scarcely sensible to criticise them for being coercive; rather, the only real issue is whether they are more coercive than they need to be.
Inevitably, the answer to that question depends on an assessment of the facts. If one believes that Covid is not a serious disease, then any restriction on the unvaccinated is plainly unjustifiable. And much the same would hold were vaccination ineffective or unduly dangerous. But if one accepts the Doherty Institute report and the latest modelling by the US Centres for Disease Control, the picture is altogether different.
Those studies suggest that the unvaccinated are about five times more likely than the vaccinated to catch Covid. Even assuming that the unvaccinated and the vaccinated transmit the disease at exactly the same rate, that implies that adding just one unvaccinated person to a group of five people who are vaccinated doubles the risk of there being a person in the group who has Covid.
Clearly, the overall danger that poses depends on the likelihood that the infection will be passed on, seeding a major outbreak; the higher the proportion of the population that is vaccinated, and the better the quality of contact tracing and isolation, the lower must be the probability of an uncontrolled outbreak.
But even putting aside the possibility of further spread, the fact that about 7 per cent of the symptomatic cases among the vaccinated are sufficiently serious as to require hospitalisation provides some basis for policy measures that limit the scope for contagion by restricting the unvaccinated’s access to crowded places. And where the contagion would affect very vulnerable groups – as in aged-care homes or in hospitals – the case for restricting access by the unvaccinated is overwhelming.
Those measures may seem harsh but they are hardly discriminatory: far from treating like unalike (which is the essence of discrimination), they differentiate on the basis of a crucial difference, namely the risk of infection.
However, the function of the restrictions is not to simply reduce the immediate likelihood of transmission; it is also to encourage the unvaccinated to seek vaccination. They therefore resemble punishments, which, when taken to an extreme (as in “no jab, no school” policies), can make vaccination virtually mandatory.
Presumably, the goal being pursued is that of achieving a level of vaccination in the community that is sufficient to reach a degree of herd immunity, reducing the need for costly lockdowns.
Just how high that level has to be is controversial; what is certain is that any gains from herd immunity, once it is achieved, flow to both the vaccinated and unvaccinated, in terms of reduced risk of illness and improved quality of life. And because the unvaccinated stand a far higher chance of catching the disease and dying from it, they derive a substantially greater benefit than do the vaccinated from herd immunity being reached.
To that extent, the unvaccinated free-ride on a benefit they have done nothing to secure, other than by making a trivial contribution to the fiscal cost of the vaccines. It could be argued that their free riding on the risks and inconvenience borne by the vaccinated causes no harm, as the benefit remains intact, at least in the short term; but it is reasonable to suggest that a free society will struggle to survive if the principle of mutual obligation can be lightly disregarded, including when it comes to sharing the cost of vital public goods. Rather, just as refusing to be conscripted in times of war should not come cheaply, so the refusal to be vaccinated when one readily and safely could ought to come with hurdles and burdens attached.
Of course, it may be that imposing any such penalties will prove unnecessary. Perhaps the intense fear of Covid that has gripped Australia will make very high levels of vaccination easy to achieve. But even if it did, there would still be a risk that when the vaccinations have to be renewed, the passage of time will have dulled the panic, causing herd immunity, and the benefits it brings, to be lost. Having lived in fear for the past two years, wouldn’t it be ironic if now all we had to fear was the waning of fear itself?
CREDIT: Henry Ergas

Subject: Infections; Restrictions; Torts; Disease; Herd immunity; Libertarianism; Epidemics

____________________________________________________________

Herald Sun

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 16 Sep 2021: 20.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/herald-sun/docview/2572709503/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Herald+Sun&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Herald+Sun&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=20&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Reward earned for jab success VICTORIANS have at last some sense of progress, even if it’s yet to materialise in much-needed and immediate lockdown relief.
Today, the state is expected to achieve its 70 per cent first vaccine dose milestone across the 16-plus population — a full week before the original estimate of September 23.
Victoria has also now crossed the one million jab target through state vaccine hubs — four days earlier than the five weeks needed that was originally forecast.
The signs are well and truly there that Victorians, and increasingly younger cohorts in their 20s and 30s, are coming forward in greater numbers to access not just the health protections of vaccination but the end to lockdowns the jabs promise.
Today, Premier Daniel Andrews is likely to announce minor changes to the distance people can travel from home, 10km instead of 5km, and an extra hour of outdoor exercise with perhaps small tweaks to child minding for essential workers.
For millions of beleaguered Melburnians enduring their sixth numbing lockdown, the fringe changes will mean little or nothing.
Interestingly, in a sign of the government’s belated recognition of the social and emotional costs of lockdowns, Premier Andrews indicated some “social” restriction amendments may be included in the small-step reliefs today, if possible.
Of course, the Andrews government’s road map for greater business, travel and private life easings in the period between now and when we hit the nationally-endorsed 70 per cent double-dose trigger for an end to lockdowns is due to be announced on Sunday.
This road map, informed by Burnet Institute modelling on projected Covid infection and hospitalisation rates, will be a plan that shows whether the Andrews government is now responding not just to Covid case numbers but the full spectrum of by-product costs to industry, education and community of wholesale shutdowns for months on end.
Nonetheless, a risk-averse balance is still raising questions about how proportional the health advice and responses are, with Ballarat yesterday thrust back into lockdown for a week despite having only four positive cases, although multiple wastewater detections back to September 8. The yo-yo open and shut mandates would be especially hard to take given lockdown was lifted for the regional city just six days ago.
Certainly, Delta is not as manageable as Alpha of the second-wave Wuhan strain seen last year: it transmits at least twice as fast, is hospitalising up to 10 per cent of cases and is primarily now hitting the young, mobile and unvaccinated.
The response to it must reflect the reality of both its risk, hospital load pressures and the need to live with it as mass vaccination rates increase.
Yet the western and northern corridors of Melbourne, and now emerging concerns around Dandenong — where case loads are driving the outbreaks — show vaccine access and the messaging program are still not hitting the required marks. Of Victoria’s 423 new Covid cases yesterday, 274 were in the north and 112 in western suburbs. Amid those regions, there was at least some better news with Hume, Wyndham and Melton all lifting in their first-dose jab rates by more than 30 per cent — albeit from low bases.
With an estimated one in eight cases in recent times linked to the construction sector, a highly mobile and young workforce, there needs to be better safe-work compliance to stem spread.
The wide use of rapid antigen testing in all essential workforces is needed as a priority to get ahead of Delta instead of continually chasing it as clusters occur at sites or within industries.
Water focus for city MELBOURNE has never made the most of its inner-city harbour and iconic Yarra River.
Correction: in times long past we used to make much more of our waterways, particularly the early 20th century Henley-on-Yarra regattas that could attract 300,000 people — much of the city — in a colourful pageant for boating and picnics.
And of course the Wurundjeri people had for millennia lived, fished and gathered around the Birrarung, cherishing the river as a place of cultural significance.
In recent decades, as Southbank developed and Northbank remains a work in progress, the Yarra has again become more a focal point. To the west, Docklands redrew the CBD footprint and took it to the edge of a repurposed Victoria Harbour.
Today, the Herald sun reports a new Melbourne ferry terminal for Docklands will aim to be “as popular as Circular Quay in Sydney.’’ Perhaps forget the Sydney comparisons and instead make it something uniquely our own.
“Better yet, it could be the Flinders Street Station of water transport services,’’ Deputy Mayor Nicholas Reece said.
The passenger terminal seeks to boost ferry services and tourism to Geelong and the Bellarine Peninsula. As we re-enliven the city post-pandemic, new visions like this are to be embraced.

Push for Cup fans as Dan mulls plan

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 13 Sep 2021: 11.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/push-cup-fans-as-dan-mulls-plan/docview/2571757921/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Push+for+Cup+fans+as+Dan+mulls+plan&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Push+for+Cup+fans+as+Dan+mulls+plan&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=11&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: EXCLUSIVE CAULFIELD Cup organisers want thousands of vaccinated racegoers trackside at the popular race next month, as Premier Daniel Andrews promises to detail a reopening plan within days.
Amid growing calls for more hope and certainty for Victorian businesses, families and schoolchildren before term 4, Mr Andrews said on Sunday he would release a comprehensive plan “in about a week’s time”.
“That will give people a sense of schools, hospitality, where they can go, who they can visit, all of those things,” he said.
It comes as Melbourne Racing Club prepares to submit a proposal for up to 7500 spectators at the October 16 event in what is hoped could signal a safe return of sporting crowds.
Chief executive Josh Blanksby told the Herald Sun the club was willing to ensure every fan was doubled vaccinated, wore a mask, showed proof of vaccination, placed bets from their seats and used QR codes around the course.
“We can do all those things,’’ he said. “If Health came back to us and said ‘these are the requirements we need’ … we could do betting in seats, seated for food, obviously masks, check-ins for all our restaurants, we could do it all.” The state government will this week receive more Burnet Institute modelling on the effect of easing restrictions as vaccination targets are hit. The Premier has previously suggested a very minor easing of Melbourne’s lockdown, including an extension of the 5km travel limit to 10km, when the state hits a 70 per cent single dose vaccination rate – projected to be September 17.
On Sunday, he said that any “roadmap in that race to 80 per cent” would include caveats based on the number of active cases and people in hospital.
He also said it would include better ventilation at schools and staggered entry times for students, but refused to commit to a term 4 date when students would return to the classroom. It comes as Opposition Leader Matthew Guy called for the government to consider “social interaction bubbles”, allowing small gatherings between families, in time for the AFL grand final on September 25.
“The government has got a couple of weeks now to prepare for it,” he said. “I hope they do because Victorian families deserve it. They have earnt that.” It comes as Sydney pushes to have crowds back at The Everest on the same day as the Caulfield Cup despite having far more Covid-19 infections.
The NSW government last week announced 5000 people would be allowed at racetracks once 70 per cent of the state was fully vaccinated.
Mr Blanksby said it would be “the definition of irony’’ to have spectators in Covid-plagued Sydney and none in Melbourne. He said he would “push right up to the end’’ to have spectators in the stands.
More than 90 per cent of Victorians aged 16 and over are projected to have had one dose by Caulfield Cup day, according to Covid Live. The state is then projected to hit the 70 per cent fully-vaccinated target on November 16 and 80 per cent on December 8.
Melbourne Racing Club originally wanted 30,000 fans across four zones for the Caulfield Cup but will now lobby for one area of up to 7500 fans.
While it hopes for a decision by the end of the month, Mr Blanksby said it could host fans at a week’s notice. peter.rolfe@news.com.au
CREDIT: EXCLUSIVE PETER ROLFE SARAH PERILLO

'Show us the health advice on NSW reopening plan': Plibersek

Publication info: The Canberra Times ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 13 Sep 2021: 0.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/show-us-health-advice-on-nsw-reopening-plan/docview/2571636114/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=01576925&amp;isbn=&amp;title=%27Show+us+the+health+advice+on+NSW+reopening+plan%27%3A+Plibersek&amp;jtitle=The+Canberra+Times&amp;pubname=The+Canberra+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=%27Show+us+the+health+advice+on+NSW+reopening+plan%27%3A+Plibersek&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=0&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: The NSW government should reveal advice from its health authorities about plans to ease COVID restrictions at 70 per cent vaccination rates, Labor MP Tanya Plibersek has said.
Ms Plibersek called for greater transparency from Gladys Berejiklian about the competing advice the state government received on reopening, after the NSW Premier announced last week she would stop daily press conferences on the coronavirus outbreak.
"I would be much more confident about the 70 per cent target if it was clear that the Premier was getting health advice that backed it, and that it was clear that those other things that the Doherty modelling talks about, like tracking, tracing, the capacity of our hospital system to cope with the number of sick people, if those things were taken care of," Ms Plibersek told ABC's Insiders on Sunday.
"We're not going to get answers to those questions because the Premier has suspended Parliament and cancelled the daily press briefings that she was giving."
A reopening plan unveiled last week showed the NSW government would lift stay-at-home orders for fully vaccinated adults from the Monday after the state passes the 70 per cent double dose target.
Up to five visitors will be allowed in a home where all adults are vaccinated, and hospitality venues, retail stores, gyms and other services and venues will be able reopen subject to spacing requirements, while domestic travel, including trips to regional NSW, will be permitted.
It has been reported that the NSW chief health officer backed a more cautious plan for reopening at 80-85 per cent vaccination coverage, to avoid the state re-entering lockdown after easing restrictions.
READ MORE:
'This is the way': The factions and ructions in Keneally's lower house play PR firms lands $770k contract to boost Indigenous vaccination NSW reopening to hit ACT hospitals: Barr Ms Plibersek said the national reopening plan agreed by states and territory leaders last month left room for interpretation, and said Prime Minister Scott Morrison should work with first ministers as they planned to ease restrictions according to their local situations.
"We've got a Prime Minister who's urging states to open up, lift all restrictions, get on with it, but not prepared to take any responsibility for his role here," she said.
"The reason we're in this second lockdown is because Scott Morrison failed on the vaccine rollout and quarantine."
The Labor education spokeswoman backed the NSW government's decision to mandate vaccinations for teachers.
However she said state and territory governments should give teachers the opportunity to be vaccinated first before making it mandatory.
"It's really important that front-line workers get vaccinated and the real problem here is we've got thousands of front line workers including teachers and school staff who are desperate to get vaccinated, who haven't had the opportunity to do so," Ms Plibersek said.
"Ultimately, I believe if you're a front-line worker, you should be vaccinated. Teachers risk their own health, but they also risk the health of the people they go home to and the kids if they're not vaccinated."
Ms Plibersek said the nation should look at school based COVID vaccination programs for children.
She also called for the government to look for vaccine supply for younger children from companies testing vaccines for young age groups.

Data behind reopening plan 'too optimistic'

Publication info: The Age ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 13 Sep 2021: 1.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/data-behind-reopening-plan-too-optimistic/docview/2571602974/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=2203580X&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Data+behind+reopening+plan+%27too+optimistic%27&amp;jtitle=The+Age&amp;pubname=The+Age&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Data+behind+reopening+plan+%27too+optimistic%27%3A+Exclusive&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=1&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: The professor behind a model provided to the WA government that predicted scores of coronavirus deaths if restrictions are relaxed too soon says the Doherty Institute modelling on which the national reopening plan is based is "perhaps too optimistic". University of Western Australia Professor George Milne's modelling forecasts 13,390 cases, 1373 hospitalisations and 186 deaths in the six months after WA reopens. It suggests a need for vaccination coverage of 85 per cent.
REPORT Page 8
CREDIT: Liam Mannix | Science reporter

LETTERS TO THE EDITOR

Publication info: The Daily Telegraph ; Surry Hills, N.S.W. [Surry Hills, N.S.W]. 09 Sep 2021: 20.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/letters-editor/docview/2570217495/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=18360203&amp;isbn=&amp;title=LETTERS+TO+THE+EDITOR&amp;jtitle=The+Daily+Telegraph&amp;pubname=The+Daily+Telegraph&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=LETTERS+TO+THE+EDITOR&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=20&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Hope for an end to tale of two cities The NSW Treasurer is spot-on when it comes to a call — finally — on when and how we lift restrictions (FreeDom: Treasurer demands every suburb unshackled, 8/9).
“This is not a tale of two cities,” he has stated.
The pity is that is exactly what we are in now where some LGAs are hot spots and have higher levels of restriction (fair enough) than those who live in less-affected areas.
And perhaps if the government and health authorities are going to continue making this distinction, rather than look at an LGA, perhaps they could consider rules by suburb.
Where I live, my postcode includes a number of suburbs in an adjoining lower-risk LGA. I suspect that the number of Covid cases in my suburb are probably closer to those in The Hills rather than the figures for the Blacktown LGA.
If new health orders can now place restrictions on higher-risk apartment blocks (High-risk block bans, 8/9), surely the same level of delineation could be applied to suburbs.
Ray Abé, Kellyville Ridge
Pollies must stick to a united path to freedom Everywhere I look I see people ­trying to comply with lockdown provisions. In Bathurst, for example, many are queuing in freezing early morning weather to be vaccinated.
Daily they get political division and uncertainty though they crave unity, as do small businesses gasping to survive (FreeDom: Treasurer demands every suburb unshackled, 8/9).
Blame for vaccine complacency, rollout and associated problems like hospitalisation and educating children have now been done to death.
It is beyond time our politicians put aside their power-driven egos to co-operate in the interests of not just state, but national wellbeing.
We know how the north-south divide in the US adversely impacts lives, especially now with so many misled, obtuse and ignorant in the south succumbing to Covid.
If we do not want to emerge from this pandemic as the disunited states of Australia, co-operation among political and community leaders is crucial.
Ron Sinclair, Bathurst
Jab rate should be key NSW Treasurer Dominic Perrottet is calling for all of Sydney to come out of lockdown together, which ­differs from Premier Gladys ­Berejiklian.
Western Sydney doesn’t need to be in lockdown longer than other parts of the city, as vaccination ­numbers have increased in the so called hot spots.
The NSW government needs to represent all of us equally and not dish out conflicting rules about ­lockdowns, as regional areas with no reported cases have also worn the brunt of unfair decisions from ­Macquarie Street.
Michael Kovas, Homebush
Don’t ignore Doherty details Not only is the NSW Premier at odds with Treasurer Dominic Perrottet about how and when restrictions will ease, there is a much more significant clash between government and the work carried out by the Doherty Institute.
Anyone who has read the now-revised and famed Doherty report will see that it does not support the wholesale easing of restrictions even when a 70 per cent to 80 per cent vaccination rate has been achieved.
Their modelling does not account for open borders and it certainly does not take into account daily infection rates of more than 1200, 80 per cent of which unlinked.
I’m all for going back to some semblance of normality, but fear that the strain on hospitals will only get worse, because when 80 per cent of the nation is vaccinated some nine million people will ­remain unvaccinated.
Dr Jack Dikian, Mosman
Electoral backlash looms I’m glad to see that the Treasurer is in favour of all of Sydney opening up at once.
The west and southwest of Sydney both appear to have been treated very unfairly, and if the reopening is delayed out here then there could be a push-back against the government by the locals. If this doesn’t occur immediately then it will likely happen at the next election. Perhaps these areas are being punished because they are generally Labor electorates.
C. Clark, Ingleburn
Our sacrifices for nothing?
To open all the suburbs, even those that have endured tougher ­restrictions due to higher virus ­volume, would be a slap in the face for every person in NSW that’s been under one draconian law or another.
Team Berejiklian has had us all living under the jackboot of the police force, with the ADF in ­assistance, and has had us all locked up for months on end – and now the plan could be to just fling open the gates?!
Then what has this all been for?
We’ve been told ad nauseam that contact tracing is the quintessential ingredient to keeping the spread manageable, and we’ve been told vaccination is the layer that will bring us closer to freedom.
But contact tracers are already under the pump, vaccines will not stop the spread of the virus and the unvaccinated won’t enjoy the same reinstatement of their democratic rights as will the rest.
It would seem Team Berejiklian has just shown us, and the rest of the world, how to implement an exercise in futility.
Christopher Woodley, Vaucluse
Howard’s wise counsel Prime Minister Scott Morrison, ­Energy Minister Angus Taylor, NSW Premier Gladys Berejiklian, NSW Energy Minister Matt Kean and all Australians would do well to heed the wisdom of John Howard – one of our greatest prime ministers – when he ­cautions that we should ignore the UN and people that advocate ­destroying coal (Howard hits out at UN’s coal war, 8/9).
Coal is one of the most valuable export industries that this country has and it also helps lift living standards in underdeveloped countries.
Thank you, Mr Howard. You’ve offered far too much common sense for the current generation of woke ideologues and climate alarmists to comprehend.
Bob Partington, Wahroonga
Women deserve better The woke bureaucrats who decided to redefine the word “woman” for the NSW Woman of the Year awards, have completely lost the plot (Man, I feel like a woman, 8/9).
Allowing men who feel like or identify as women to potentially be named NSW Woman of the Year is not only farcical, it is also totally ­unfair to all the women across the state who are so deserving of this prestigious honour and makes a mockery of the award.
Bureaucrats who are on the public purse should get on with their jobs rather that redefining what it means to be a woman, ultimately letting down real women with their politically correct nonsense.
Thomas Carrie, Summer Hill
States don’t control ADF It is dumbfounding that there is even a question about the manner of return of ADF personnel from ­Afghanistan, or anywhere, to Queensland (Soldiers stranded, 8/9).
Surely the federal government has the power to determine how our forces are deployed and redeployed without reference to recalcitrant state government authorities.
Gary Bryant, Gladesville
Briefly...
Instead of including men who ­identify as women in the Woman of the Year awards, why don’t we set up an award for the dumbest ­bureaucrat of the year (Man, I feel like a woman, 8/9)? I’m sure there would be plenty of candidates for that position.
Jim Gardiner, New Lambton ●●● On the bright side of pub lockdowns, I have had better conversations with my fellow drinkers sitting ­outside the places where we would normally drink. They are more ­coherent, as am I.
Gary Frances, Bexley ●●● After reading stories of the galahs in some local councils since amalgamation, this could be a very good match-up (There’s a new pecking order: Council swoops on magpie, 8/9). Galah versus magpie – I’m backing the magpie.
Erwin Vitnell, Bathurst

On the wrong track with questionable calls

Publication info: Newcastle Herald ; Newcastle, N.S.W. [Newcastle, N.S.W]. 08 Sep 2021: 22.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/on-wrong-track-with-questionable-calls/docview/2569680175/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=22028021&amp;isbn=&amp;title=On+the+wrong+track+with+questionable+calls&amp;jtitle=Newcastle+Herald&amp;pubname=Newcastle+Herald&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=On+the+wrong+track+with+questionable+calls&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=22&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: letters to the editor
I REFER to the article "Sneak peek inside $2b intercity train" (Herald, 2/9) regarding the new trains being ready for service.
As an ex-railwayman with 30 years' service, I am staggered at the NSW government's decision to curtail the guard's overall safety function on the new, foreign-built trains. The whole history of these trains is bad from the beginning. When challenged about them not being built in NSW, a certain Premier replied that "the people of NSW expect value for money". Well, that didn't happen as we all know. When the new carriages were off loaded on the wharf it was quickly discovered that they were out of gauge for the lines they were required to travel on. There was then a need to shave all tunnels and platforms where these trains were destined to travel, thus increasing the overall cost. When challenged on this and again asked why the trains were not built in NSW, the Premier replied "We're not very good at building trains in NSW," thereby exposing her complete lack of knowledge of the history of railways in this state. You may remember that she was the Transport Minister who thought that it would be a great idea to tear up the railway line to the second largest city in NSW?
During my railway career I progressed through the various grades in the traffic branch, including that of guard. I completed my service as the senior safety officer, Northern Region, based at Newcastle. I do not know the chief operating officer, Mr Merrick's background and I wonder what, if any, operating experience he has with railways. The guard is responsible for the safe working of the train and is also qualified to render first aid in an emergency, as well as generally looking after the wellbeing of the passengers and answering any inquiries they may have.
The driver has the responsibility of driving the train, adhering to the signals and watching the track ahead and must be prepared to act in any emergency that may occur on the line. I believe that the current plan to take away the guard's observation of the train and passengers when stopped at a platform and replace that with a camera is foolhardy and dangerous in the extreme. NSW Railways have been a forerunner in rail safety over many decades. This current plan, if enabled, will erode that record. If I was a driver, I would refuse to move a train without the guard's "right away" as it is unsafe to do so.
Mark Gattenhof, Wyoming Winds of change are blowing THE federal government's new bill to legalise the offshore wind industry in Commonwealth water will open Australia to global investment in offshore wind farms, with thousands of jobs expected to flow into industrial towns facing an uncertain future as manufacturing continues to decline. These projects will provide cheap, clean energy for existing manufacturing and attract private investment in new export industries such as hydrogen and local wind turbine manufacturing.
Offshore wind means onshore jobs as it's cheaper to build the massive turbines close by rather than transport them from other locations. The projects also require many of the skills that workers in fossil fuel industries already have and thus building them in places where workers have those skills should be a priority. This means a Newcastle offshore wind farm could be the kickstart we need for a green industrial rebirth in the Hunter Valley. A recent economic analysis by Beyond Zero Emissions said Newcastle, with its deep water port, skilled workforce and existing industrial infrastructure could attract $28 billion in capital investment to the region, generate $11 billion in revenue by 2032 and create 34,000 jobs.
Offshore generation and transmission will deliver a more secure and reliable electricity system and create thousands of jobs and business opportunities in regional Australia. In light of the rapidly declining coal sector due to shrinking global demand for thermal coal, the Hunter Valley must be made a priority for offshore wind by the government.
Bryce Ham, Whitebridge It pays to dig a little deeper GEORGE Williams, ("Put money where your mouth is", Letters, 3/9), regarding the Premier's token pats on the back for nurses and ambos, it's much worse than you might think.
Not only did Gladys Berejiklian block a pay rise for these people this year, last year she also froze the pay of 400,000 frontline public sector workers who were helping fight the pandemic. To add insult to injury, around the same time, Ms Berejiklian increased Minister for Finance and Small Business Damien Tudehope's $309,621 salary by $17,000.
Then to further rub salt into the wound, the Premier also approved Police Commissioner Mick Fuller's request for a $87,000 pay rise (bringing his pay up to $649,500), all while he was investigating her and Scott Morrison's alleged role in allowing over 130 people infected with COVID-19 to disembark the Ruby Princess cruise ship into Sydney without quarantining (Plus at the start of this year, Commissioner Fuller was granted a further pay rise of $16,250 by Berejiklian).
I've been questioning the Premier's priorities for years now, but all of these matters truly make my jaw drop. I also think that Ms Berejiklian granting a pay rise to someone investigating her is a bit suspect to say the very least.
Adz Carter, Newcastle 'Lockouts' for the unvaccinated ACCORDING to Doherty modelling, when Australia is fully vaccinated, we are unlikely to ever celebrate a "Freedom Day" where masks are thrown away and where lockdowns end.
The rest of our lives will be spent in partial lockdowns and being vigilant against possible infection. We will need to learn to live with COVID infections and deaths. Every year, we will receive booster shots. Mask-wearing will become mandatory indoors apart from in our homes. Lockdowns will become "lockouts" for the unvaccinated at all sorts of venues. The unvaccinated will be unable to travel.
As Australia approaches full vaccination, we should brace ourselves for more violent "freedom" demonstrations by anti-vaxxers and conspiracy groups.
Geoff Black, Caves Beach How we can be heard on climate THE Australian Security Leaders Climate Group have released a grim report highlighting how ill-prepared Australia is in assessing and addressing the risks posed by climate change, such as climate-fuelled water and food insecurity.
Our defence leaders are one of the many experts in various fields who are calling on the Morrison government for leadership and urgent action to decarbonise our economy. Yet it is very frustrating to know that little has been done to protect us Australians from future climate catastrophe.
We, as individuals, may not be able to control the actions of our politicians and the policies they set. However, we do control the outcome of the ballot box at the next federal election. If Scott Morrison and the Coalition party do not bring in the strong climate policies needed to protect us Australians from future damage, then I suggest that we vote them out.
Ching Ang, Kensington Gardens
CREDIT: Letters to the editor

Australia's vaccine supply issues are over: Frewen

Publication info: The Canberra Times ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 08 Sep 2021: 4.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/australias-vaccine-supply-issues-are-over-frewen/docview/2569679241/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=01576925&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Australia%27s+vaccine+supply+issues+are+over%3A+Frewen&amp;jtitle=The+Canberra+Times&amp;pubname=The+Canberra+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Australia%27s+vaccine+supply+issues+are+over%3A+Frewen&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=4&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Australia's vaccine supply issues are over and the success of the nation's rollout now hinges on public uptake, the man tasked with leading it says.
COVID-19 Taskforce Commander Lieutenant-General John Frewen has also told Australian Community Media that vaccination rates among Indigenous Australians must be accelerated to avoid leaving them exposed once restrictions begin to ease.
The early stages of Australia's rollout were wracked by an undersupply of Pfizer, a problem compounded by the Australian Technical Advisory Group on Immunisation advising against the use of AstraZeneca in people under 60.
But with an imminent influx of mRNA vaccines, General John Frewen said Australia was on track to ramp up its rollout in September, a month earlier than initially expected.
"This takes us beyond the phase in the vaccine [rollout] where we were really mRNA supply constrained. The vaccines are coming, the distribution networks are in place and expanding. It all comes down now to public willingness to come forward," he told Australian Community Media.
"Sentiment remains strong and high, which is pleasing. But I'm not counting any chickens until we've got everybody through the door.
"We've still got a ways to go to get us to 70 per cent, and international experience tells us you've got work hard between 70 per cent and 80 per cent."
READ MORE COVID-19 NEWS:
Barr warns young people against taking 'unnecessary risks' Landlords who reduce rents will get lower tax bills Barr hopes Christmas spend can help ACT's economy rebound The federal government last week confirmed it had agreed a vaccine swap with the UK for four million Pfizer doses, the first batch arriving on Sunday. Australia would return the doses by December, General Frewen predicted.
It followed a similar agreement with Singapore for 500,000 doses, and the federal government purchasing an additional one million from Poland.
General Frewen said the deals, coupled with the imminent availability of the Moderna jab, would allow the country's remaining GPs to administer mRNA vaccines.
"[That] is going to help us ramp up through September in ways that we didn't think would be possible until October," he said.
"We've got every opportunity, provided people keep stepping forward. So I really encourage everybody who hasn't made a booking to get in and make one now."
The nation's reopening plan, based on modelling by the Doherty Institute, saw Australia begin to reopen once 70 per cent of each state and territory was fully-vaccinated.
That did not include 12- to 15-year-olds, who were all eligible to make a booking from Monday.
READ MORE COVID-19 NEWS:
War memorial building site listed as COVID exposure location ACT gets new COVID treatment In the Delta wave, who will think of the children? Nor did it include a minimum threshold for Indigenous and Torres Strait Islander Australians, raising the prospect of Australia opening up while they remained vulnerable.
Just over 40 per cent of Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Australians aged 16 and over had received a first dose by Monday, well below the broader population of 63 per cent. Just 23 per cent were fully-vaccinated, compared to 38 per cent nationally.
General Frewen conceded vaccination among First Nations peoples had fallen behind during the initial stages of the rollout and, although it had doubled in the last month, the rate was still only keeping pace with the rest of Australia.
"So we've got to accelerate it to catch it up, because we don't want them to be behind when the rest of the nation gets to 70 and 80 per cent," he said.
"We're going to accelerate where we can over the next month by focusing on some of those communities that are most behind right now."
General Frewen would not predict when the Indigenous population would hit 70 per cent, warning vaccine hesitancy would prove the "ultimate variable". He warned a relatively COVID-free existence in most remote communities, some of which had vaccination rates under 10 per cent, had caused complacency over the vaccine.
But the virus' spread through regional NSW, including the first deaths among Indigenous Australians, had prompted a shift in the region. Work was being done with local leaders and trusted community identities to counter disinformation, he said.
"[They are] stressing not only that the vaccines are safe and important, but there is a real urgency to this," he said.
"Western NSW has given us a good reminder that COVID can rip into these communities really quickly, so we're better to get in and get ahead of the curve."
READ MORE COVID-19 NEWS:
Buying more time to defeat Delta comes at a hefty cost Sources for some cases in Covid outbreak may never be identified Pfizer vaccine bookings opened to Canberrans aged 16 to 29 General Frewen said ATAGI's advice on AstraZeneca had particularly impacted Indigenous Australians, who were more likely to be aged under 60. That was complicated by logistical challenges posed by Pfizer, which needed to be transported and stored at freezing temperatures.
And as a September 17 deadline for all residential aged care workers to receive at least one dose loomed, General Frewen insisted Australia was on track to vaccinate the vast majority of the cohort.
Eighty-five per cent had received a first dose and 64 per cent were fully-vaccinated, with roughly 40,000 still requiring a first dose, he revealed.

NSW backflips on aged-care patients

Publication info: The Courier - Mail ; Brisbane, Qld. [Brisbane, Qld]. 07 Sep 2021: 6.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/nsw-backflips-on-aged-care-patients/docview/2569471911/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=13225235&amp;isbn=&amp;title=NSW+backflips+on+aged-care+patients&amp;jtitle=The+Courier+-+Mail&amp;pubname=The+Courier+-+Mail&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=NSW+backflips+on+aged-care+patients&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=6&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: HEALTH authorities in NSW have reversed a decision directing aged-care homes to care for Covid-positive residents on site amid a shortage of hospital beds, after at least two facilities were told there was not enough space for non-critical residents to be transferred into care.
It comes as new modelling predicted that up to 947 people would require intensive care treatment in hospital by the middle of October, creating as “overwhelming impact” on ICU operations.
Aged-care facilities initially told to manage Covid cases on-site include the Uniting Edinglassie Lodge in Penrith, where nine residents tested positive to Covid last week.
In a statement on Friday, Uniting said seven residents had been transferred to hospital, but the facility was advised by NSW Health that no further clinically stable residents would be transferred.
By Monday, an “agreement” had been reached to transfer the remaining two residents to hospital, according to a Uniting statement.
NSW chief health officer Kerry Chant on Monday said decisions about transferring aged-care residents with Covid to hospital was made by a “case-by-case assessment … (considering) what’s in the best interest of the facility”. Sydney’s second wave is predicted to grow at a rate of up to 2500 cases per day by mid September.
CREDIT: James O’Doherty; David Barwell

NSW backflips on aged-care patients

____________________________________________________________

Stop fear-mongering and get with the plan

Publication info: The Gold Coast Bulletin ; Southport, Qld. [Southport, Qld]. 06 Sep 2021: 8.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/stop-fear-mongering-get-with-plan/docview/2569274959/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=13213830&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Stop+fear-mongering+and+get+with+the+plan&amp;jtitle=The+Gold+Coast+Bulletin&amp;pubname=The+Gold+Coast+Bulletin&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Stop+fear-mongering+and+get+with+the+plan&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=8&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: NOT content with manipulating the fears of older Queenslanders, the Premier has now moved to frightening parents.
In a desperate attempt to deflect criticism from her border decisions, Palaszczuk said that any opening of the border before “every child (is) vaccinated” was putting children at risk.
“You open up this state and you let the virus in here and every child under 12 is vulnerable, every single child.” Not only does this conflict with the scientific evidence, it also sets a requirement for opening that no government can ever reach.
At present, 7 per cent of people over 70 who contract Covid will end up in ICU, with 46 per cent needing hospitalisation for a less severe set of symptoms.
In contrast, even for the Delta strain, just 0.1 per cent of people under 14 who contract it will find themselves in ICU.
This is why a long line of medical experts has advised against the need to vaccinate children under 12.
Queensland paediatrician Professor Robert Booy says “keeping borders shut because of the children is not a sensible measure”. He makes it very clear that the risk exists only for the small number of children who are already complex cases because of existing chronic conditions.
Professor Fiona Russell, paediatrician with the Murdoch Children’s Research Institute, has explained that while children can exhibit flu-like symptoms, they rarely become seriously ill, even with the Delta strain. As she put it, “Covid in kids is nothing like what occurs in adults.” And in responding to Palaszczuk’s fear-mongering, Professor Paul Kelly – Australia’s chief medical officer – said that even with the Delta variant, there is “very little evidence anywhere in the world that severity has increased” for childhood infections.
Using NSW as an example, Professor Kelly explained that most of the children hospitalised for Covid reasons were there because their parents were sick and couldn’t look after them.
What Palaszczuk neglected to mention was that there is no vaccine recommended for children under 12 – not in Australia or another country. This means she’s setting a requirement for opening the borders that no government can reach.
This overreach exposes what has been at the heart of her motivation for months now: using fear to maintain power and control.
It was one thing to lock Queenslanders in when there was no vaccine, but Australians now have access to vaccines in abundance. To continue to lock up Queenslanders indefinitely – even when 70 or 80 per cent of the community is vaccinated – is plainly unreasonable. Yet this is what the Premier is calling for by suggesting we must wait until a vaccine for kids has been invented, tested, manufactured and distributed. No such vaccine is even on the horizon.
What’s worse is the Premier has no alternative plan to hiding under the doona for the foreseeable future. Snap lockdowns and closed state borders – with their dubious constitutionality in an environment of abundant vaccines – are no recipe for confronting and dealing with this virus.
The Doherty modelling is clear. At 70 and 80 per cent vaccination, the harms that come to physical and mental health exceed the risk that comes from Covid itself.
Federal Labor agrees with the national plan. Asked about Palaszczuk’s comments, Labor leader Anthony Albanese said “we need to follow the health advice”. Deputy leader Richard Marles said “I would be distancing myself from the comments of Anastacia.” It’s time for the Premier to stop toying with the health, safety, livelihoods, and economy of Queenslanders. Time for her to stop undermining confidence in the vaccine, and confidence in the National Plan without an alternative vision of her own.
Rather than playing games, the Premier should focus on getting Queensland’s hospitals up to scratch. Ramping rates at hospitals are a far bigger risk to children with complex and chronic conditions than Covid.
There’s only one plan on the table for safely moving out of the pandemic and confidently resuming normal life, giving our children confidence of continuity in their education, job stability and business viability. The economic support measures cannot go on forever – and it would be corrosive to our national morale if they did.
It’s time for the Queensland government to honour the commitment it made at National Cabinet in July, and get serious about implementing the National Plan. Amanda Stoker is an LNP Senator for Queensland
CREDIT: AMANDA STOKER

Subject: Vaccines; Fear & phobias; Children & youth

Location: Australia Queensland Australia

JOURNOS SPLIT ON PARTY LINES

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 06 Sep 2021: 20.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/journos-split-on-party-lines/docview/2569273862/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=JOURNOS+SPLIT+ON+PARTY+LINES&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=JOURNOS+SPLIT+ON+PARTY+LINES&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=20&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: For all the media criticism of politicians’ handling of Covid-19, history may judge our leaders to have performed better than journalists and medical regulators.
Media sensationalism played a big role in hyping a one-in-a-million risk of death from rare blood clots associated with Australia’s preferred AstraZeneca vaccine. Coverage was so overblown many people in Sydney are still waiting for Pfizer jabs, rather than take the readily available Astra. Yet ABC health editor Norman Swan, an early critic of Astra, now says it may be more effective than Pfizer against the Delta variant of Covid.
Our medical regulators also harmed the vaccine rollout. The Therapeutic Goods Administration’s first approval came six weeks after US approval. Then the Australian Technical Advisory Group on Immunisation stoked vaccine hesitancy by first “preferring” Astra for over 50s, and then for over 60s. This left Australia with globally low vaccination rates in June at the start of the present Sydney outbreak.
Hindsight is easy but Astra was the logical choice for Australia a year ago. Labor would probably have made the same choice. Astra could be stored at normal refrigeration temperatures, was easier to handle and distribute and could be made in Australia.
As this column argued three months ago, Prime Minister Scott Morrison had little choice, with almost no Covid at the time, but to cave in to ATAGI’s “medical advice”, given the ABC and others would have blamed him personally for every Astra blood clot death. Morrison did eventually find his mettle in June when Delta hit Sydney, and on cue he was bagged by the ABC and Guardian for urging young people to see their doctor about an Astra jab.
Media coverage of virus escapes from quarantine has been just as flawed. Journalists should know hotel quarantine was agreed to by national cabinet. Victorian Premier Dan Andrews even claimed it was his idea. The premiers understandably wanted the money from quarantine to support CBD hotels that were struggling with low occupancy rates after the international border closure.
Morrison has largely ignored his critics on vaccination and quarantine to preserve the unity of national cabinet. This leaves him looking flat-footed as Labor premiers backflip on deals they have already agreed to. Few reporters call the premiers out. For example, journalists who could not give Labor state and federal politicians enough space to demand faster quarantine returns of Australians stuck overseas never questioned the premiers mid-year when they demanded dramatic reductions in overseas arrivals.
The truth is politicians did not know what they did not know at the start of the pandemic. Yet they got a fair bit right. From the decision to stop flights between Australia and China to the closing of our international borders and the introduction of JobKeeper, our leaders did a better job than most overseas governments. They got half a million Australians home and finished 2020 with among the world’s lowest Covid death rates – 910 dead from a population of 25 million – and the second best performing economy in the OECD.
The media did less well last year. Journalists jumped all over the Berejiklian government’s mishandling of the Ruby Princess in April 2020. Partisan reporters tried to pin it on then Home Affairs Minister Peter Dutton. An inquiry by the nation’s sharpest Queen’s Counsel, Bret Walker, pinned most of the blame on state health officials.
The media again split along partisan lines over the 112-day lockdown by Victorian Premier Dan Andrews, who held his own Commission of Inquiry that ended in the resignation of the head of his department and claims no one knew who decided on private security firms to run the state’s hotel quarantine.
The media Right went nuts, and still is on Andrews. The media Left fell in behind the “I Stand With Dan” hashtag on Twitter. Journalists who asked Andrews tough questions were targeted personally on social media. No journalist should “stand with” any politician, until they leave the business for PR.
Many journalists who defended every step Andrews made last year have criticised every Berejiklian step this year. They don’t mention Victoria’s much higher death rate. Nor do they admit reporters from this newspaper and Sky News have been just as tough on Berejiklian as Andrews.
By the middle of last week even the most provincial media defenders of the Labor premiers prepared to abandon their own national cabinet commitments in the name of Covid-zero must have been starting to see the light. Andrews on Wednesday admitted Victoria’s hard lockdown would not achieve zero Covid.
He was accepting what Berejiklian had said for more than a month – only rapidly increasing vaccination would pare back the Delta virus. WA and Queensland Labor leaders Mark McGowan and Annastacia Palaszczuk will eventually reach the same conclusion. Journalists who can’t see the obvious just need to look at New Zealand’s latest outbreak.
Palaszczuk and her chief health officer disgraced themselves mid-year with false claims about AstraZeneca. They did so again last week when they allowed the families of NRL players across the state’s border even as they refused entry to a three-year-old who had been holidaying with his grandparents in NSW. As politics, it put Sir Joh Bjelke-Petersen to shame. Palaszczuk’s fearmongering on Doherty Report modelling in state parliament last week rivalled Craig Kelly for misinformation.
Worse have been the senior ABC women feeding into false claims about vaccination for children. Palaszczuk falsely implied Coalition governments were risking child safety when she knows young children are not approved for vaccination anywhere in the world. While they can catch Covid, it seldom makes them sick.
Neither has a timid, politically correct media been intelligent enough to look seriously at the cultural factors in Aboriginal Australia and in outer suburban Sydney and Melbourne multicultural communities that feed vaccine hesitancy and rapid case spread.
My Aboriginal contacts have been predicting a Covid disaster in outback communities all year. Cultural, family and lifestyle issues, more than poverty, racism or language, have assured the most vaccine-hesitant communities in Aboriginal and multicultural communities are also the ones with the highest smoking rates, the worst general health, the highest density per household and the highest rate of multi-generational families.
That sort of reporting is difficult. It’s much easier to lead bulletins with sensational claims of soaring case numbers, out-of-control virus spread and a failing hospital system, even though this is not what is happening.
Sydney’s hospital system is coping well. Lockdowns in Sydney and Melbourne have slowed the spread. Virus reproduction numbers are falling and in Sydney, it’s only just above 1. Case numbers in Sydney and Melbourne will continue to rise, but the curve will bend as vaccinations rates rise. More interesting will be watching how the health systems cope in WA and Queensland when Delta inevitably hits the way it did in New Zealand.
CREDIT: Chris Mitchell

Subject: Native peoples; Vaccines; Journalists; Reporters; Quarantine; Multiculturalism & pluralism; Immunization; Hospital systems; COVID-19

Children in ICU as patients increase

Publication info: The Daily Telegraph ; Surry Hills, N.S.W. [Surry Hills, N.S.W]. 06 Sep 2021: 9.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/children-icu-as-patients-increase/docview/2569273839/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=18360203&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Children+in+ICU+as+patients+increase&amp;jtitle=The+Daily+Telegraph&amp;pubname=The+Daily+Telegraph&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Children+in+ICU+as+patients+increase&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=9&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: THREE children under 13 – including an infant – are among 175 NSW Covid patients in ­intensive care.
NSW Health confirmed on Sunday that a baby, a nine-year-old and a 12-year-old were admitted to the ICU as a result of their Covid diagnosis.
All of the children have underlying health conditions.
Health officer Dr Jeremy McAnulty said the three children in ICU were “in context” of approximately 2000 people being cared for by the Children’s Hospital network.
NSW reported 1485 new Covid cases on Sunday and three deaths. The deaths included a 50-year-old woman with no obvious underlying health conditions who had received one vaccine dose.
Premier Gladys Berejiklian is set to release modelling as early as today showing case numbers peaking in the next two weeks, before hospitalisations increase into October.
It comes after authorities again pushed back a deadline for residents of hotspot local government areas to have at least one vaccine dose in order to leave their area for work.
Those residents will now have until September 19 to get their first dose of vaccine if their job requires them to leave their LGA.
It is the second time the vaccination deadline has been pushed back and comes amid claims from unions that thousands of critical staff would be prevented from going to work because they have not been able to book their first dose.
All authorised workers will need to have made a booking by Wednesday to get their jab before the new deadline.
Meanwhile, Federal Health Minister Greg Hunt has announced that vulnerable unvaccinated Australians will be individually approached by GPs to get a jab.
Mr Hunt said as the country edged towards 70 per cent first-dose coverage, the nationwide push to vaccinate “pockets of disadvantage” would ensure the most vulnerable groups were protected ahead of reopening.
New information released by NSW Health has also revealed that of the Covid cases reported in the week to August 21, more than 10 per cent had been admitted to hospital.
The hospitalisation rate of 11 per cent is almost double the figure offered by Premier Gladys Berejiklian last week, when she said the “most recent figure … was five and a half per cent”.
The Premier’s office could not say when the Premier was advised of a 5.5 per cent hospitalisation rate.
Sixty additional paramedics will hit the roads from September 11 in an attempt to bolster the faltering NSW Ambulance workforce. But NSW Health Services Union secretary Gerard Hayes said that figure is less than half of what is required.
CREDIT: James O’Doherty; Clare Armstrong; Angira Bharadwaj
