Letters

Publication info: The Courier - Mail ; Brisbane, Qld. [Brisbane, Qld]. 26 Aug 2021: 46.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/letters/docview/2564310597/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=unknown&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=13225235&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Letters&amp;jtitle=The+Courier+-+Mail&amp;pubname=The+Courier+-+Mail&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Letters&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=46&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: BREWING BORDER WARS THE Lethbridge cartoon (C-M, Aug 25) depicting states as a bunch of cars pulling in opposite directions was on the money.
It’s the latest in a series of pictorial, written and spoken statements highlighting the threat to Australia’s wellbeing posed by over-powerful state governments.
What’s emerging in the current crisis is an incredible state-centred selfishness.
Watching the current Queensland-NSW border imbroglio, it’s almost as if a civil war has broken out.
The saying a house divided against itself cannot stand comes to mind. Just imagine if our nation was at war with some insidious foe. What’s that you say? It already is?
At any rate, the big question is: how can the federal government take back sufficient power from the states to protect the nation as a whole, when an existential crisis such as Covid-19 strikes?
Paul Dobbyn, Wynnum
IT IS understandable that Premier Palaszczuk stays cagey on the plan to open up Queensland to the rest of the country under the Doherty Institute modelling in the face of rampant Covid-19 cases in New South Wales (C-M, Aug 25).
Nobody wants to live in lockdown.
The rampant virus in New South Wales could cause a lockdown in Queensland at any time.
The priority is to keep Queensland open and safe for Queenslanders. The pathway out to border openings is vaccinations, built quarantine and personal hygiene including mask wearing.
The Morrison government is responsible for vaccinations and quarantine. Action by the Morrison government on vaccinations and quarantine, rather than words is the real light at the end of the tunnel back to life as we knew it.
The Palaszczuk government has earned trust in keeping Queenslanders safe by its actions.
The Morrison government must now earn trust by its actions.
Out of trust, hope will be born.
David Muir, Indooroopilly
WE WILL have to live with Covid forever, it’s not going away. We will need yearly vacs and have the info on your phone. So let’s get on with it, get vaccinated and back to living with open borders.
Frankie Little, Daisy Hill
SMART POLLIES FACE TEST PETER Gleeson’s recent article (C-M, Aug 23) on prospective politicians requiring a minimum IQ level before being endorsed to contest a seat, certainly has merit.
The problem associated with this requirement, however, is trying to find the people with the required IQ level who are willing to take on the job for the salary offered.
This statement will certainly raise the ire of many people who consider politicians to be grossly overpaid.
But consider this, people with an IQ rating well above the normal, are generally employed in positions paying well above the level of politicians.
Would any of them be prepared to drop a significant amount of annual remuneration to take on the dubious honour of being a member of parliament? Not likely.
So the situation will remain the same until the remuneration package equals the expectations of any aspiring politician.
As the old saying goes, if you pay peanuts you get monkeys.
Phil Stoneham, Middle Park
PETER Gleeson’s article on the IQ of MPs, has raised some interesting remarks. The parliament should be a microcosm of the electorate.
That there is an over representation of a particular segment is the fault of the political parties who select candidates based on a particular basis.
Senate committees are interesting and sometimes frivolous but just because a senator does not have a degree does not say that they are of less IQ than those with degrees.
Quite often it is proved that the most intelligent and the ones asking the most penetrating questions are those who are the quiet achievers.
Don’t judge a person by their qualifications, but by their actions, then you’ll know who the muppets are!
Lin Powell, Craignish
STANCE ON YOUTH CRIME FAR northern judge, Justice James Henry, has spoken for the people and the way people feel (C-M, Aug 25).
By revoking the bail of a repeat 15-year-old juvenile offender, he has given a glimmer of hope that more judges might follow his lead.
The community should be first and foremost in their decision making. These recidivist offenders have little if any fear of the consequences and yet the public often live in fear of where these juveniles might strike next.
Such crime has been rampant in recent months and, if the Labor government will do little about it, rulings by judges of Justice Henry’s ilk must become commonplace.
Ken Johnston, Rochedale South
MENTAL HEALTH MEASURES I CONFESS to considerable scepticism when conservative voices talk about the impacts of lockdown on mental health.
These are the same people who pursued the fundamentally flawed Robodebt scheme, even after they were told of the damage it was doing.
And they threw people into detention centres for the crime of fleeing persecution, then ignored the professional advice on suicide and self-harm.
If conservatives are concerned for the mental wellbeing of people they can immediately act to bring about the following changes.
First, release all refugees and asylum seekers from institutional incarceration, as recommended by mental health experts.
Next, implement all of the findings of the Royal Commission into Aboriginal Deaths in Custody, as recommended by mental health experts.
Last, provide a four-fold increase in funding for veterans’ reintegration programs, as recommended by mental health experts.
Let’s see real support for critical mental health programs, and not ideological posturing that will sell out at the first convenient opportunity.
Stephen Morgan, Carina Heights
VALE CHARLIE WATTS I HEARD the sad news about the passing of Charlie Watts, drummer of the Rolling Stones who played with the band for nearly 60 years.
What a talent and gentleman he was, often seen donning a three-piece suit while Mick and the rest were in jeans and T-shirts.
We love your work.
His wife Shirley once famously said: “Oh Charlie isn’t really a Rolling Stone he just plays on tour with the band now and then”.
So very glad that he did. RIP dear old Charlie hope you remembered to take your drumsticks to heaven.
Margo Campbell, Birkdale
WHEN MAGPIES ATTACK WHAT good news that we have an expert on magpies at Griffith University. I wasn’t aware that was a course that was available and one has to wonder how long it takes to complete – and what use it actually is.
This expert must live on a different planet to me.
I have never yet seen a magpie fly around in front of a human to see if they recognise the face of a person before attacking.
In my experience, unlike plovers that I have seen attack from the front, magpies always attack from behind.
Syd Cutmore, Golden Beach
WAKE UP CRAIG IF CRAIG Kelly thinks that he will be the leader and sole formulator of policy for The United Australia Party I’ve got a block of flood-prone land to sell him.
The only person who will be leading that party is Clive Palmer. Forget about “Wake up Jeff”.
It’s “Wake up Craig!” Cherie Alexander, Runcorn

Subject: COVID-19 vaccines; Mental health; Coronaviruses; Immunization; Politics; Quarantine

Location: Australia Queensland Australia New South Wales Australia

TEXT TALK

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 25 Aug 2021: 27.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/text-talk/docview/2563920245/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=TEXT+TALK&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=TEXT+TALK&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=27&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: SCOTT Morrison has a plan for living, Andrews’ plan is for control. Anthony John, Box Hill
GREAT to see ScoMo stand his ground on moving out of lockdown with 80 per cent vaccination. I now want to see him act on it when the time comes, including holding back funding to WA, Queensland and Victoria.
George, Kew
MODELLING by the Doherty Institute is exactly that, modelling. It is based on computer simulation. How close does it resemble real life?
Jacko, Balwyn
POLLIES want to get on top of the virus at any cost … except it’s not costing them.
Paul, Rosanna
CAN someone please tell me why there is a wait of six weeks before I can get the first doze of Pfizer, and why we don’t have 24/7 clinics open, to cope with demand?
Gary, Sunbury
GOT my first dose of Pfizer through a vaccination hub, came time to book my second and my local GP said if you didn’t get your first one here, we can’t do the second. How is that right and how does that help the rollout?
Ron, Beaconsfield
WILL Minister Foley publish the medical advice saying public hospitals do not need to Covid test before admissions? RMH situation is another debacle.
Cheryl, Elwood
MONTHS ago all who were going into hospital for ops were tested two days before day of operation. I would like to know why this is not being done now.
Angela, Hampton Park
THERE are 1476 beds in ICUs in Victoria and just nine Covid patients admitted there. Where is the “state of emergency”, Professor Sutton?
Mario Moldoveanu, Frankston
48,424 tests yesterday, 48,374 negative results. Good work, Victorians. This arrogant, fearmongering Andrews government can’t be positive, so I will.
Damo, Koo Wee Rup
YES, Susie O’Brien (Opinion, 24/8), Dan’s “zero fixation” and making us cave dwellers is to keep us in the dark so he can keep feeding us BS.
Gigi, Christmas Hills
BRILLIANT letter by Adrian Sexton (YV, 24/8). That is what most rural and regional people are saying and thinking but getting the message through to this dictator is hopeless.
Neil, Morwell
THANKS Tom (50/50, 24/8) for common sense. This government is so self-righteous. The most incompetent ever.
Angela, South Morang
ABC … A Buttrose Calamity? Ross Dyson, Harrys Creek

STREWTH

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 25 Aug 2021: 2.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/strewth/docview/2563918996/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=STREWTH&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=STREWTH&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=2&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Vax on, vax off Here’s one for the diary. The National Audit Office — of #sportsrorts and #carparkrorts fame — has launched an inquiry into the vaccine strollout and is set to report back by April. If Scott Morrison fulfils his promise to be a “full-termer”, the ANAO’s analysis could be released before the next federal election (due by May 21, 2022). “The objective of this audit is to assess the effectiveness of the planning and implementation of the Covid-19 vaccine rollout,” the ANAO said, hinting at a focus on Greg Hunt’s Health Department. The jimmy jab games began in February, with the government initially planning for all Quiet Australians to receive both doses by October. NSW hit the six million milestone on Tuesday, with Gladys Berejiklian promising fully vaxxed punters a TBC treat. There’s plenty for the auditors to delve into: supply and sourcing (Poland); delays and eligibility (to give and receive); Vaxzevria (aka AstraZeneca) hesitancy and indemnity; Dolly Parton’s Moderna; private school students jumping the queue and private consultants (PWC’s secret $1m a month contract); translations that were weeks out of date; political tattoos exposed during shots (e.g. Peter Gutwein’s black panther); the useless $10m CovidSafe app; boots on the ground; Thor refusing to star in a pro-vax ad; and a definitive answer on which Paul Kelly we should take our health advice from. The ANAO is accepting submissions until January 30, 2022. If they have time, could they look at delay and supply issues with toilet paper during periods of panic-buying?
Crood culture The Prime Minister’s latest road map to recovery is based on a fictional prehistoric family who live in a cave. You know what they say, it’s always darkest before the Delta dawn. “It’s like that movie, The Croods,” Morrison waxed comical. “Some wanted to stay in the cave, and that young girl, she wanted to go out and live again and deal with the challenges of living in a different world. Well, Covid is a new, different world, and we need to get out there and live in it. We can’t stay in the cave.” His review? “I like the movie.” No doubt temporary The Lodge resident Josh Frydenberg is breathing a sigh of relief he’s dropped “under the doona” metaphors.
Coming out of my cave For those unfamiliar, The Croods is based on a story by John Cleese and made $23.8m at the Aussie box office in 2013. It stars Nicolas Cage (in a cave!), Emma Stone, Ryan Reynolds and Catherine Keener. Strewth had a spare 94 minutes before question time so we took some animated PG notes. The Quiet Australian neanderthals –Grug, Eep, Ugga, Thunk, Sandy and Gran – live in a cave (lockdown) without light. State premier Grug is stubborn and overprotective so institutes a ring of steel and curfew. The Croods’ neighbours die from natural selection: smashed by a mammoth, swallowed by a snake, mosquito bite and the common cold (the UK’s Freedom Day). Rebellious daughter Eep (Morrison) meets inventive orphan Guy (Doherty Institute), who has a pet sloth named Belt (Dan Murphy opening hours). Guy can make fire (modelling) and warns of the impending apocalypse (double dip recession). An earthquake (Delta) destroys the cave, the Croods flee to find a new cave (in-principle agreements). The family escape a volcanic eruption (anti-vaxxers who punch horses) and learn to live outside in a tropical paradise (70 per cent vaccination). The end! If only The Croods had fled earlier and harder, they could have reached the sun sooner! Morrison told the Coalition partyroom there’s a sequel (The Crood 2: A New Age), and he hopes for his own sequel next year when we heads to the polls. As Grug philosophised: “No one said survival was fun.”
Jibber jabber Remember: it’s not a (fast food) race. “There’s more places you can get the vaccine in Australia then you can get a Big Mac now,” ScoMo claimed on Tuesday. Even in Engadine?
Head in the game Scott Morrison has taken a job as the new leader of the Perth Wildcats. No, not that Scott Morrison. Coach ScoMo is a 44-year-old Canadian, and former assistant at the Boston Celtics. He’s signed a three-year NBL contract to replace head coach Trevor Gleeson, who is moving to man the whistle for Canada’s Toronto Raptors. Coach ScoMo will be the Wildcats 14th head coach in its 40-year history, with 10 winning seasons. PM ScoMo is the Liberal Party’s 14th leader in its 76th-year history, with 19 election wins. Coincidence? “Over the years, it’s been kind of funny to get a lot of people hitting me up on Twitter, cursing at me and complaining about me,” coach ScoMo reflected. “I’m not really sure what they were upset about. If the shoe was on the other foot and my favourite Canadian team hired Justin Trudeau to coach the team, I think I’d get a great kick out of that, so I don’t blame anybody for laughing.” The self-described “players coach” hopes he can keep top spot on the ScoMo popularity poll, but with his second child due this weekend could use a favour from his namesake before the season starts on November 18. “If the other Scott Morrison can help me cut down my quarantine, I’m happy to step down and give him my vote,” he joked. Maybe he should ask Mark McGowan. So does PM ScoMo play ball? Continuing the parable of political sporting fails (see: John Howard, cricket), Morrison once posted a video playing basketball with his skilled daughter Abbey in 2019. After missing the hoop, he turned to the camera and said: “Needs a bit of work”. Sure … but has coach ScoMo seen The Croods? You can’t play basketball inside a cave.
Love it or list it David Littleproud has scored a coveted 10 out of 10 from Rate My Skype Room. Run by Jessie Bahrey and Claude Taylor from Washington DC, the Twitter account shot to fame at the start of the pandemic for its humorous voyeurism of the backdrops used in televised interviews, virtual parliaments, celebrity livestreams and online events around the world. The armchair critiques are fairly simple in format, focusing on the set-up and lighting, with particular attention paid to the WFH rooms that resemble hostage videos. “Nice grouping of urns/ceramics/porcelain. Twigs. Art. Doing rural Australia proud,” RMSR said of the deputy Nationals leader’s Ryeford residence. Littleproud thanked the interior interpreters, adding: “I only wish it was me who designed it!!!! (Brownie points at home for this tweet but!).” We’re reliably informed full credit should go to his partner, Amelia. strewth@theaustralian.com.au
CREDIT: ALICE WORKMAN

Subject: Vaccines; Remakes & sequels; Professional basketball; Immunization; COVID-19

Location: Australia

Title: STREWTH

Publication title: The Australian; Canberra, A.C.T.

First page: 2

Publication year: 2021

Publication date: Aug 25, 2021

Section: TheNation

Publisher: Nationwide News Pty Ltd

Place of publication: Canberra, A.C.T.

Country of publication: Australia, Canberra, A.C.T.

Publication subject: General Interest Periodicals--Australia

ISSN: 10388761

Source type: Newspaper

Language of publication: English

Document type: News

ProQuest document ID: 2563918996

Document URL: https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/strewth/docview/2563918996/se-2?accountid=13552

STREWTH

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 25 Aug 2021: 2.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/strewth/docview/2563918293/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=STREWTH&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=STREWTH&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=2&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Well, Covid is a new, different world, and we need to get out there and live in it. We can’t stay in the cave.” His review? “I like the movie.” No doubt temporary The Lodge resident Josh Frydenberg is breathing a sigh of relief he’s dropped “under the doona” metaphors.
Coming out of my cave For those unfamiliar, The Croods is based on a story by John Cleese and made $23.8m at the Aussie box office in 2013. It stars Nicolas Cage (in a cave!), Emma Stone, Ryan Reynolds and Catherine Keener. Strewth had a spare 94 minutes before question time so we took some animated PG notes. The Quiet Australian neanderthals –Grug, Eep, Ugga, Thunk, Sandy and Gran – live in a cave (lockdown) without light. State premier Grug is stubborn and overprotective so institutes a ring of steel and curfew. The Croods’ neighbours die from natural selection: smashed by a mammoth, swallowed by a snake, mosquito bite and the common cold (the UK’s Freedom Day). Rebellious daughter Eep (Morrison) meets inventive orphan Guy (Doherty Institute), who has a pet sloth named Belt (Dan Murphy opening hours). Guy can make fire (modelling) and warns of the impending apocalypse (double dip recession). An earthquake (Delta) destroys the cave, the Croods flee to find a new cave (in-principle agreements). The family escape a volcanic eruption (anti-vaxxers who punch horses) and learn to live outside in a tropical paradise (70 per cent vaccination). The end! If only The Croods had fled earlier and harder, they could have reached the sun sooner! Morrison told the Coalition partyroom there’s a sequel (The Crood 2: A New Age), and he hopes for his own sequel next year when we heads to the polls. As Grug philosophised: “No one said survival was fun.”
Jibber jabber Remember: it’s not a (fast food) race. “There’s more places you can get the vaccine in Australia then you can get a Big Mac now,” ScoMo claimed on Tuesday. Even in Engadine?
Head in the game Scott Morrison has taken a job as the new leader of the Perth Wildcats. No, not that Scott Morrison. Coach ScoMo is a 44-year-old Canadian, and former assistant at the Boston Celtics. He’s signed a three-year NBL contract to replace head coach Trevor Gleeson, who is moving to man the whistle for Canada’s Toronto Raptors. Coach ScoMo will be the Wildcats 14th head coach in its 40-year history, with 10 winning seasons. PM ScoMo is the Liberal Party’s 14th leader in its 76th-year history, with 19 election wins. Coincidence? “Over the years, it’s been kind of funny to get a lot of people hitting me up on Twitter, cursing at me and complaining about me,” coach ScoMo reflected. “I’m not really sure what they were upset about. If the shoe was on the other foot and my favourite Canadian team hired Justin Trudeau to coach the team, I think I’d get a great kick out of that, so I don’t blame anybody for laughing.” The self-described “players coach” hopes he can keep top spot on the ScoMo popularity poll, but with his second child due this weekend could use a favour from his namesake before the season starts on November 18. “If the other Scott Morrison can help me cut down my quarantine, I’m happy to step down and give him my vote,” he joked. Maybe he should ask Mark McGowan. So does PM ScoMo play ball? Continuing the parable of political sporting fails (see: John Howard, cricket), Morrison once posted a video playing basketball with his skilled daughter Abbey in 2019. After missing the hoop, he turned to the camera and said: “Needs a bit of work”. Sure … but has coach ScoMo seen The Croods? You can’t play basketball inside a cave.
Love it or list it David Littleproud has scored a coveted 10 out of 10 from Rate My Skype Room. Run by Jessie Bahrey and Claude Taylor from Washington DC, the Twitter account shot to fame at the start of the pandemic for its humorous voyeurism of the backdrops used in televised interviews, virtual parliaments, celebrity livestreams and online events around the world. The armchair critiques are fairly simple in format, focusing on the set-up and lighting, with particular attention paid to the WFH rooms that resemble hostage videos. “Nice grouping of urns/ceramics/porcelain. Twigs. Art. Doing rural Australia proud,” RMSR said of the deputy Nationals leader’s Ryeford residence. Littleproud thanked the interior interpreters, adding: “I only wish it was me who designed it!!!! (Brownie points at home for this tweet but!).” We’re reliably informed full credit should go to his partner, Amelia. strewth@theaustralian.com.au
CREDIT: ALICE WORKMAN

Strewth!

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 25 Aug 2021: 2.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/strewth/docview/2563917789/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Strewth%21&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Strewth%21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=2&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Vax on, vax off Here’s one for the diary. The National Audit Office — of #sportsrorts and #carparkrorts fame — has launched an inquiry into the vaccine strollout and is set to report back by April. If Scott Morrison fulfils his promise to be a “full-termer”, the ANAO’s analysis could be released before the next federal election (due by May 21, 2022). “The objective of this audit is to assess the effectiveness of the planning and implementation of the Covid-19 vaccine rollout,” the ANAO said, hinting at a focus on Greg Hunt’s Health Department. The jimmy jab games began in February, with the government initially planning for all Quiet Australians to receive both doses by October. NSW hit the six million milestone on Tuesday, with Gladys Berejiklian promising fully vaxxed punters a TBC treat. There’s plenty for the auditors to delve into: supply and sourcing (Poland); delays and eligibility (to give and receive); Vaxzevria (aka AstraZeneca) hesitancy and indemnity; Dolly Parton’s Moderna; private school students jumping the queue and private consultants (PWC’s secret $1m a month contract); translations that were weeks out of date; political tattoos exposed during shots (e.g. Peter Gutwein’s black panther); the useless $10m CovidSafe app; boots on the ground; Thor refusing to star in a pro-vax ad; and a definitive answer on which Paul Kelly we should take our health advice from. The ANAO is accepting submissions until January 30, 2022. If they have time, could they look at delay and supply issues with toilet paper during periods of panic-buying?
Crood culture The Prime Minister’s latest road map to recovery is based on a fictional prehistoric family who live in a cave. You know what they say, it’s always darkest before the Delta dawn. “It’s like that movie, The Croods,” Morrison waxed comical. “Some wanted to stay in the cave, and that young girl, she wanted to go out and live again and deal with the challenges of living in a different world. Most workers support vaccination passports

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 19 Aug 2021: 5.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/most-workers-support-vaccination-passports/docview/2562348261/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Most+workers+support+vaccination+passports&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Most+workers+support+vaccination+passports&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=5&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: More than 70 per cent of workers support the rollout of a vaccine passport, while less than a quarter believe employers should mandate vaccinations for staff, new polling has revealed.
The survey of 999 Australian employees by global HR software firm Employment Hero found overwhelming support for providing greater freedoms for fully vaccinated citizens, including almost 77 per cent in NSW.
A range of business groups has called for the use of vaccine certificates in Australia, but several ­Coalition MPs have voiced concerns about their requirement for interstate travel and attending venues and events.
The Employee Covid-19 Vaccination Sentiment Report found 71 per cent of employees surveyed support the use of green cards – already used in Israel and the EU. More than two-thirds felt vaccine passports were necessary to protect family health and get businesses operating again.
Only 16 per cent of surveyed workers said they were against the concept. Victoria, Tasmania and the Northern Territory are currently working on vaccine passport pilot programs and will deliver recommendations to nat­ional cabinet.
Under national cabinet’s vaccination road map – based on modelling conducted by the Doherty Institute – a 70 per cent threshold will see the use of lockdowns and hard border closures curtailed, with draconian restrictions almost disappearing once 80 per cent of the population is vaccinated.
West Australian Premier Mark McGowan has already announced that travellers from Covid hotspots – a new “extreme high risk” category – will be required to show proof they have had at least one jab.
Six per cent of surveyed workers said they had been actively discouraged from getting the ­vaccine by their workplace. Employees in microbusinesses (two-nine staff) were 198 per cent more likely to report employers were pushing them not to get the jab.
More than half of Australian workers in the survey believed the federal government had mismanaged the vaccine rollout; 57 per cent of workers agreed the federal government vaccine messaging was confusing.
Moreover, more than 60 per cent of workers surveyed said their workplace had little influence on their decision to be vaccinated, with more than half saying their employer had never spoken to them about getting the vaccine. Only 22 per cent of those surveyed said employers should mandate vaccinations for staff.
Employment Hero chief executive Ben Thompson said ongoing uncertainty about staff vaccination meant employers were “terrified of doing the wrong thing”. Of those who said they wouldn’t get vaccinated, 12 per cent said they were against the vaccine. More than a quarter were waiting for mRNA vaccines and 24 per cent said they were waiting until vaccines had been available for longer.
CREDIT: MAX MADDISON

NSW, the iso state

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 16 Aug 2021: 8.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/nsw-iso-state/docview/2561325384/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=NSW%2C+the+iso+state&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=NSW%2C+the+iso+state&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=8&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: NEW South Wales is expected to be isolated from the rest of the country for months to come, as state leaders acknowledge its lockdown will never drive case numbers back to zero.
The worsening outbreak could throw into chaos the national cabinet’s agreed plan to reopen Australia, because it assumed there would be only small number of Covid-19 cases when the vaccination targets of 70 per cent and 80 per cent were reached.
The state recorded 415 cases on Sunday as Gladys Berejiklian said it was “not possible to eliminate it completely”.
“The best chance we have to live with it freely and safely is to get the case numbers down as low as possible,” the NSW Premier said.
Victorian Premier Daniel Andrews agreed, saying there was “no way they’re going to get to zero cases”.
“Nothing else they’re doing is working … This is where they’re at now, they’re going to have to vaccinate their way out of this,” Mr Andrews said.
He said there was “no doubt” about the national cabinet’s plan to end lockdowns and reduce restrictions among state and territory leaders.
“No one’s walking away from the plan,” Mr Andrews said.
But senior federal government sources said that for the national cabinet plan to succeed, they expected states would keep their borders closed to NSW for months, and possibly until Christmas.
Mr Andrews said that if Ms Berejiklian reopened her state at the 80 per cent threshold, and there were still many cases of community transmission, then “they’ll be locked out to here”.
He said the Doherty Institute modelling underpinning the national cabinet plan was based on Australia reaching the vaccination thresholds while having successfully suppressed the virus.
“That modelling does not assume that there are thousands of cases, and hundreds of cases every day,” Mr Andrews said.
“If we want to be open, if we want to have a new set of rules as we go into a new year, if we want to preserve our health system for everyone who needs it … then when we get to 70 and 80, we have to have cases down here, not raging.
“Otherwise I don’t know how that works.” Mr Andrews maintained that was why it was paramount that Melbourne’s continuing lockdown was successful, because the city and state could not reopen if it had thousands of cases, even with 80 per cent of people over 16 fully vaccinated.
“I’m determined to make sure that it doesn’t ­happen here,” Mr Andrews said. With all of NSW now in lockdown, chief health officer Kerry Chant said on Sunday: “I cannot stress enough the seriousness of the current situation, and my grave concerns that these case numbers will continue to escalate.” tom.minear@news.com.au
CREDIT: Tom Minear

Children and Delta

Publication info: The Press ; Christchurch, New Zealand [Christchurch, New Zealand]. 14 Aug 2021: A.12.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/children-delta/docview/2560865132/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=01139762&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Children+and+Delta&amp;jtitle=The+Press&amp;pubname=The+Press&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Children+and+Delta&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.12&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Amid reports of spikes in children being hospitalised for Covid-19 overseas, New Zealand experts explain why they have 'no new concerns' for youngsters here. Brittney Deguara reports.
Despite Covid-19 infections and hospitalisations spiking in children in parts of the world, one leading paediatric infectious disease specialist in New Zealand sees "no new concerns" with the Delta variant and children younger than 16.
In the United States, 93,824 children - defined here as younger than 20 years - tested positive in the week ending August 5, making up about 15 per cent of confirmed cases. To put this into perspective, the week ending July 22 saw 38,654 new cases in that age group.
Associate Professor Tony Walls, a paediatric infectious disease specialist from the University of Otago, explained there were more cases in children simply because there was more transmission in the wider community.
"I don't think anyone thinks that there's more severe disease occurring in children because of the Delta variant, but obviously it's pretty early and people are keeping a close eye on that."
The US Centres for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), for example, is actively investigating the potential for this variant to cause severe disease in adults and children.
Dr Nikki Turner, director of the Immunisation Advisory Centre, said there was some indication 12 to 15-year-olds were contracting the Delta variant more severely, but it wasn't affecting them as badly as older age groups.
Walls, however, said data from the UK suggested there was "no new concerns" for children under 16 and how they might be affected by the Delta variant.
In England, at the end of July, an estimated 1.72 per cent of children from 2 to 11 years tested positive, while an estimated 3.21 per cent between 12 and 16 years tested positive. The month prior, these figures sat at 0.91 per cent and 1.25 per cent, respectively.
Children were also being hospitalised with Covid-19. In the week ending August 1, there were 3.12 hospitalisations per 100,000 for children aged 0 to 4, and 1.08 for those aged 5 to 14.
Despite this, Walls said it was still "less likely" kids would be hospitalised with severe illness.
"The risk of a child having disease severe enough to lead to a hospital admission is very, very low compared to an adult.
"My colleagues in the UK are generally reassured that there are still relatively few children that get admitted to hospital with Covid - that was the case during the first and early waves and seems to be the same even with the Delta variant spreading throughout the UK. Generally that's pretty reassuring."
The number of hospitalisations of children in the US was also increasing, but that was on par with the increase in cases, according to Dr Anthony Fauci, chief medical adviser to the US president.
"More infections mean more children will be in the hospital," he said at a recent White House media briefing.
Hospitalisations for children under 17 in the US increased by 27.3 per cent, on average, in the week ending August 10. The week prior there had been an average 46.3 per cent increase.
One recent pre-print study out of Uruguay's University of the Republic, which was yet to be peer- reviewed, estimated age-specific frequencies of severe, critical and fatal disease. It found children up to 9 years had a 0.094 per cent chance of getting severely ill, a 0.008 per cent chance of being critical, and practically no chance (0.00048 per cent) of dying from the virus. For the 10 to 19-year age bracket, the risk increased - 0.2 per cent for severe disease, 0.021 per cent for critical disease, and 0.0018 per cent for fatal disease.
Some had raised concerns about reopening New Zealand's borders and the effect it might have on children under 16 who were yet to be vaccinated.
Amanda Kvalsvig, a senior research fellow in the department of public health at the University of Otago, previously told Stuff planning to vaccinate children as young as 12 needed to happen "right away".
But vaccinations were less likely to have that great of an impact on that demographic, according to Australian research. With or without vaccinations for 12 to 15-year-olds, cases would still likely be reported in that age group if outbreaks emerged when the borders reopened. Some may even be severe.
"Even though younger people are less affected, when many people get the disease then you'll get some who will get it more severely," Turner said.
She described it simply as a "number's game".
Some groups of children and teenagers were more at risk of hospitalisation than others, according to Dr Jin Russell, a developmental paediatrician in Auckland, and PhD candidate in epidemiology.
Those with co-morbidities - such as lung, heart or neurological diseases, or who were obese - were at a greater risk, as were children in rural communities, Maori and Pacifika children, and those from socio-economically disadvantaged backgrounds.
The overall risk of severe outcomes for kids, however, would likely remain "very low", Walls said. There was an exponential increase in risk as people got older. So the youngest children had the least chance of getting severely ill or dying from Covid-19.
Modelling from Australia's Doherty Institute, which was provided to the Australian federal government, found vaccinating 12 to 15-year-olds "would not materially change the expected overall health outcomes" of the population. With 80 per cent of the eligible Aussie population vaccinated, jabbing teens between 12 and 15 would reduce the transmission potential of the Delta variant for all adults by just 0.1 per cent.
Turner said in terms of reaching herd immunity, vaccinating teenagers was "only a very small gain".
Speaking from an equity point of view, Turner said it would be good to vaccinate all family members at the same time. But priority still needed to be given to the vulnerable populations.
Walls agreed: "I think vaccines for 12 to 15-year-olds are a good idea for their individual protection. As a country, I think we should be focusing on vaccinating the elderly and the more vulnerable in our population before we move on to vaccinating the 12 to 15-year-olds.
"That's going to have a much greater impact in terms of reducing severe disease and illness rather than any other intervention."
Other interventions were still necessary, however. Kvalsvig highlighted the importance of minimising the risk to unvaccinated teenagers at school by monitoring C02 levels in classrooms and opening windows if levels rise, having good ventilation and filtration systems, and wearing masks.
"There are a number of measures that we can start with now to minimise children re- breathing each other's air."

Subject: Infectious diseases; Hospitals; COVID-19 vaccines; Age groups; Coronaviruses; Pediatrics; Teenagers; Disease transmission

Delta 'Super Six' blitz

Publication info: The Transcontinental ; Port Augusta, S. Aust. [Port Augusta, S. Aust]. 13 Aug 2021.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/delta-super-six-blitz/docview/2560347960/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=07293992&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Delta+%27Super+Six%27+blitz&amp;jtitle=The+Transcontinental&amp;pubname=The+Transcontinental&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Delta+%27Super+Six%27+blitz&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Who's being targeted, and why
Tasmania is about to embark on its four-point Delta Shield plan, aimed at ensuring the state is as ready as it can be ahead of any future cases of the highly infectious COVID strain.
Aimed at learning from the challenges facing NSW, Victoria and Queensland, the plan includes an aggressive vaccine blitz over the next six weeks, with the goal of boosting the state's vaccination levels past 60 per cent by mid-September.
Dubbed the Super Six campaign, a significant part of this will be targeted at college students aged 16 to 18 years old, along with additional clinics for those aged 30 to 59.
So why is Public Health targeting this group, and why now?
We've broken down the latest advice and details of the strategy aimed at ensuring Tasmania is ready for the worst case scenario.
Why blitz now? A week on from Tasmania's first new case of COVID-19 in more than seven months - confirmed to be Delta - Premier Peter Gutwein said the strain was challenging communities right across Australia's Eastern seaboard, and creating serious health and economic challenges.
While Tasmania's vaccine program continues to lead the country, he said in the face of the Delta strain, more needs to be done.
Deputy Premier and Health Minister Jeremy Rockliff said the timing of a targeted campaign to vaccinate college students, starting from August 23, had also been selected in conjunction with the Education Department to best suit examination scheduling.
Health Department Secretary Kathrine Morgan-Wicks (pictured) said they had been working to determine the right time to bring the state's college population into the vaccine roll-out, with the Health Department to offer logistical support to colleges, with assistance from school nurses already familiar with student populations.
The six-week blitz will also target those aged 30 to 59, with state community clinics set to open on extra days and for extended hours.
By lifting vaccination rates in this age group, the aim is to also bring forward the vaccination of those aged 19 to 29 in state clinics from September 13.
Why target these age groups? Mr Rockliff said the target age group, particularly 30 to 39-year-olds, had been shown by recent modelling to be a key factor in the ease of transmission of the Delta variant.
"That is why we are going to do a very big push to get this age range vaccinated," he said.
Public Health Director Dr Mark Veitch explained the latest recommendations from the Doherty Institute identified priority groups in the community - mainly older teenagers to 40-year-olds - as being more mobile in the community, and therefore posing a higher risk for transmission.
"They are the people we know from the experience of other jurisdictions, who contribute disproportionately to the spread of disease," he explained.
Ms Morgan-Wicks said the Super Six campaign would also focus on those aged up to 59.
"We are watching the percentage of vaccination rates for those age groups, and I would really like to see particularly for our 40 to 49s, our 50 to 59s, those rates to really increase," she said.
"We are probably sitting in the 40 to 50 per cent, for those groups, for first dose vaccinations. We really need to lift those up."
Do we have enough vaccines? Ms Morgan-Wicks said for the next six weeks, Tasmania had enough vaccines "to do a really big lift", to get the state above a 60 per cent vaccination rate, for those aged over 16.
"We are currently talking to the Commonwealth, and will examine the potential of whether we do need to bring forward any vaccines," she said.
She said Public Health teams were working through the numbers and making the necessary adjustments to allow for the Super Six campaign.
"It's absolutely critical that we take this time, particularly while we do have these border arrangements with both NSW and Victoria, to really focus on vaccinating," she said.
"We do have the stock. I'm confident we have the supply. We also have a very good relationship with the Commonwealth team, to bring forward supply if we need to have that conversation."
What else is involved? Community pharmacists are also expected to play a major role in the vaccination blitz.
This includes re-examining the remaining pharmacists already authorised and approved to administer COVID-19 vaccines, but who sit outside designated postcodes authorised under state guidelines.
Ms Morgan-Wicks said these decisions would also need to be matched to the supply of vaccine expected to arrive in the state.
From Monday, 11 pharmacies will start administering first and second dose AstraZeneca to over 60s aimed at filling the gap for that age group, as state clinics have moved to Pfizer only.
Mr Rockliff said they were continuing to work with the Commonwealth to bring on more pharmacies, and would look at the expansion of vaccine types and age ranges as the roll-out progresses.
"The Tasmanian guidelines reflect the fact that AstraZeneca is the only vaccine available from the Commonwealth for community pharmacies," he said.
"The TGA advice for state's like Tasmania, where there is no widespread threat of community transmission, is that AstraZeneca is the preferred vaccine for the over 60 age group."
What about 20-year-olds? Those aged 19 to 29 who don't have underlying health conditions or other eligible circumstance, are now the only approved age group who haven't been targeted in Tasmania's vaccination roll-out.
Dr Veitch said this was a gap that would need to be prioritised "once we have enough vaccines to be confident that we can open up bookings to that group".
Mr Gutwein said if the state succeeded in lifting vaccination rates for those eligible in the 30 to 59 age bracket, bookings could open to other age groups by September.
What's the end goal? In short, the goal is to have as many Tasmanians vaccinated as possible in the next six weeks. To date, more than 50 per cent of eligible Tasmanians have received one dose of a COVID-19 vaccine, and more than 28 per cent are fully vaccinated.
The Super Six plan aims to boost the state's vaccination rates well into the 60 per cent range, with the goal of also reaching 70 and 80 per cent targets more quickly.
"We will try to push as far as possible into it by mid September," Ms Morgan-Wicks said. "We had previously talked about targets for our 70 per cent, and 80 per cent. We are currently re-working our numbers on that, but trying to bring those forward as quickly as we can."
Mr Gutwein previously said he was hopeful Tasmania could reach a vaccination rate of 80 per cent by the end of November.
"I want to remind all Tasmanians that we are at a very critical point in our COVID preparedness and response," he said.
"Please continue to do the basic important things we can all be doing to stay on top of COVID."
CREDIT:

Subject: College students; Public health; Drug stores; COVID-19 vaccines; Age groups; Coronaviruses; Immunization; Disease transmission

Business indexing term: Subject: Drug stores

Location: Australia Tasmania Australia

Company / organization: Name: AstraZeneca; NAICS: 325412

Identifier / keyword: Medicaments Congresses Research

"We are watching the percentage of vaccination rates for those age groups, and I would really like to see particularly for our 40 to 49s, our 50 to 59s, those rates to really increase," she said.
"We are probably sitting in the 40 to 50 per cent, for those groups, for first dose vaccinations. We really need to lift those up."
Do we have enough vaccines? Ms Morgan-Wicks said for the next six weeks, Tasmania had enough vaccines "to do a really big lift", to get the state above a 60 per cent vaccination rate, for those aged over 16.
"We are currently talking to the Commonwealth, and will examine the potential of whether we do need to bring forward any vaccines," she said.
She said Public Health teams were working through the numbers and making the necessary adjustments to allow for the Super Six campaign.
"It's absolutely critical that we take this time, particularly while we do have these border arrangements with both NSW and Victoria, to really focus on vaccinating," she said.
"We do have the stock. I'm confident we have the supply. We also have a very good relationship with the Commonwealth team, to bring forward supply if we need to have that conversation."
What else is involved? Community pharmacists are also expected to play a major role in the vaccination blitz.
This includes re-examining the remaining pharmacists already authorised and approved to administer COVID-19 vaccines, but who sit outside designated postcodes authorised under state guidelines.
Ms Morgan-Wicks said these decisions would also need to be matched to the supply of vaccine expected to arrive in the state.
From Monday, 11 pharmacies will start administering first and second dose AstraZeneca to over 60s aimed at filling the gap for that age group, as state clinics have moved to Pfizer only.
Mr Rockliff said they were continuing to work with the Commonwealth to bring on more pharmacies, and would look at the expansion of vaccine types and age ranges as the roll-out progresses.
"The Tasmanian guidelines reflect the fact that AstraZeneca is the only vaccine available from the Commonwealth for community pharmacies," he said.
"The TGA advice for state's like Tasmania, where there is no widespread threat of community transmission, is that AstraZeneca is the preferred vaccine for the over 60 age group."
What about 20-year-olds? Those aged 19 to 29 who don't have underlying health conditions or other eligible circumstance, are now the only approved age group who haven't been targeted in Tasmania's vaccination roll-out.
Dr Veitch said this was a gap that would need to be prioritised "once we have enough vaccines to be confident that we can open up bookings to that group".
Mr Gutwein said if the state succeeded in lifting vaccination rates for those eligible in the 30 to 59 age bracket, bookings could open to other age groups by September.
What's the end goal? In short, the goal is to have as many Tasmanians vaccinated as possible in the next six weeks. To date, more than 50 per cent of eligible Tasmanians have received one dose of a COVID-19 vaccine, and more than 28 per cent are fully vaccinated.
The Super Six plan aims to boost the state's vaccination rates well into the 60 per cent range, with the goal of also reaching 70 and 80 per cent targets more quickly.
"We will try to push as far as possible into it by mid September," Ms Morgan-Wicks said. "We had previously talked about targets for our 70 per cent, and 80 per cent. We are currently re-working our numbers on that, but trying to bring those forward as quickly as we can."
Mr Gutwein previously said he was hopeful Tasmania could reach a vaccination rate of 80 per cent by the end of November.
"I want to remind all Tasmanians that we are at a very critical point in our COVID preparedness and response," he said.
"Please continue to do the basic important things we can all be doing to stay on top of COVID."

____________________________________________________________

We will need an inquiry to learn from rollout mistakes

Publication info: The Canberra Times ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 07 Aug 2021: 30.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/we-will-need-inquiry-learn-rollout-mistakes/docview/2558631998/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=01576925&amp;isbn=&amp;title=We+will+need+an+inquiry+to+learn+from+rollout+mistakes&amp;jtitle=The+Canberra+Times&amp;pubname=The+Canberra+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=We+will+need+an+inquiry+to+learn+from+rollout+mistakes&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=30&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: We are living simultaneously in two Covid worlds.
On the one hand we're talking about how life will be when 70 per cent or 80 per cent of eligible people are fully vaccinated.
On the other, what we can call our third wave of Covid is spreading, hitting young people, infecting children, resisting efforts at suppression. Sydney is in dreadful shape, NSW regions are under threat, south-east Queensland is shuttered, as is Victoria, and the rollout remains beset by difficulties.
We must, of course, have the conversation about exiting the pandemic. We need to consider issues including how a vaccine passport would work, and when home quarantine can kick in.
"Transition" and "campaign" plans abound - from national cabinet and rollout tsar Lieutenant-General J.J. Frewen. All good, but there's a pie-in-the-sky element about them when we're moving forward so slowly.
Unfortunately, but perhaps inevitably, Frewen this week found himself caught on the political fly paper, after Anthony Albanese made his populist call for everyone who's vaccinated by December 1 to receive $300.
The government launched a massive attack on on this "bubble without a thought". Frewen was dragged in because he's canvassed various incentives. His position seems to be: possibly some cash, but not now. Both sides invoked his name in making their cases for and against the Labor proposal.
Morrison is using Frewen as a political shield, just as he once used former chief medical officer and now Health Department secretary Brendan Murphy.
This has brought claims Frewen is being politicised, a perception the general needs to avoid, because it could make him less credible to the public and is bad for the military.
It would have been better if Frewen had performed his role in civvies rather than in uniform, but Morrison no doubt likes the khaki. Certainly Frewen should guard against being drawn on political questions.
The Doherty Institute modelling presented publicly on Tuesday by Professor Jodie McVernon showed how the rollout's limitations have undermined our fight against the Delta variant and will continue to do so.
Its message was that the superspreaders are those aged 20 to 40. As McVernon said, they infect both their children and their parents.
But they've been the worst catered for in the rollout. They were initially placed at the back of the queue, after the most vulnerable, key workers, and the middle-aged. And Pfizer, the vaccine preferred for them - although they're now being urged to take AstraZeneca - has been in short supply.
Belatedly, vaccinations for them are being somewhat accelerated, but it is all ad hoc and unclear.
Politicians like to talk about the "learnings" (aka lessons) from this pandemic. At some point, when we're much further down the exit road, there should be a comprehensive inquiry into how decisions were made and what went right and wrong, at both federal and state levels, particularly in the rollout but in other areas too.
In this context, Thursday's Administrative Appeals Tribunal decision that the national cabinet is not - as the federal government tried to claim - a cabinet committee and is therefore not subject to cabinet confidentiality, is a welcome development.
We can perhaps understand - while still strongly criticising - how the federal government, not expecting the problems with AstraZeneca, failed to order enough Pfizer or to have sufficient alternatives.
But how come on Thursday, when people were being shouted at to get the jab, an inefficient booking system in NSW was hampering many doing that?
And why, way back when, did the government put so much weight on the doctors in delivering the early months of the rollout? The pharmacists have only recently been brought in. If they'd been involved from near the start, we would likely be in a lot better position, at least with the AZ coverage.
The question has to be asked: how much did doctors' lobbying influence the rollout's initial shape? What clout did they have with senior health officials?
In February, the Australian Medical Association issued a statement headed "GPs, not pharmacists, best placed for vaccine rollout".
It said AMA president Omar Khorshid had written to Health Minister Greg Hunt to express the AMA's concerns.
The release went on: "Dr Khorshid told Sky News that the AMA would prefer that the rollout remained part of usual GP interactions.
"'We do have significant reservations about the place of vaccination in pharmacy,' [he said].
"'In the very, very rare occurrence of a severe reaction like anaphylaxis to a vaccine, it's something that we really can't expect a pharmacist to be able to manage.
"'But the main reason is that we think that vaccination is part of a primarily holistic care package where people have a healthcare home. They know to go and see their local GP for their healthcare needs.'"
READ MORE:
ACT Health Minister urges Canberrans not to travel in regional NSW amid COVID-19 outbreak Government rules out mandating any further workplace COVID-19 vaccinations 'On weirdness, Mr Speaker': Parliament's bad magic In the AMA's defence, this was as the program was about to get under way and reaction to the vaccines had unknown elements. But the reference to the "main reason" is a giveaway. As is sometimes said, the AMA is the country's most powerful trade union. It fights doggedly to protect its turf.
When the Coalition came to power, it launched a royal commission into the pink batts scheme. This was seen as, and was, a political exercise. Nevertheless, it did identify faults in planning and administration.
The pink batts program and its problems pale against the importance of, and the inadequacies in, the rollout.
An inquiry into the handling of the pandemic should not be driven by political motives, but rather by the need to understand the reasons for the mistakes and how to be better prepared in future.
This isn't to diminish how well, comparatively, Australia did earlier. But the good side of the record shouldn't be an excuse to avoid rigorous scrutiny of the negatives.
You won't find provision for an inquiry in the government's exit plan. But it should be there, in stage four.
Michelle Grattan is a press gallery journalist and former editor of The Canberra Times. She is a professorial fellow at the University of Canberra and writes for The Conversation, where her columns also appear.
CREDIT: Michelle Grattan

Premier's gambit: All bets on vaccination rate

Publication info: Sydney Morning Herald ; Sydney, N.S.W. [Sydney, N.S.W]. 07 Aug 2021: 24.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/premiers-gambit-all-bets-on-vaccination-rate/docview/2558442331/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=03126315&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Premier%27s+gambit%3A+All+bets+on+vaccination+rate&amp;jtitle=Sydney+Morning+Herald&amp;pubname=Sydney+Morning+Herald&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Premier%27s+gambit%3A+All+bets+on+vaccination+rate&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=24&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Rays of hope appear as patience is stretched, write Deborah Snow and Lucy Carroll.
It's been the story of this pandemic so many times before: the sun breaks through, then the clouds roll in again.
So it was at the end of this week. Just as the NSW government was gaining traction with its message to all adults to get vaccinated promptly against COVID-19 - and not to hesitate over AstraZeneca - came news that must have left the state's senior health officers with their heads in their hands.
A young woman, just 34, died from an exceedingly rare blood-clotting disorder linked to the vaccine - the sixth Australian to die from more than 6.8 million doses of AstraZeneca administered in this country.
Utterly tragic as her death is, health authorities are desperate to keep the public mind focused more on the state's mounting toll of death and disease from COVID-19, hoping this will drive vaccine uptake, the jab seemingly now the only ray of hope on NSW's horizon.
Sydney's Delta outbreak continues to smoulder at a frustratingly irrepressible rate. Thursday's figure of 262 new cases was a new high, eclipsed by yesterday's 291.
Victims are not just the elderly. Intensive care units are seeing patients in their teens, 20s and 30s. Earlier in the week, a 27-year old man died from the disease while isolating in his south-west Sydney home, the state's youngest COVID-related death to date.
Premier Gladys Berejiklian, chivvied daily by journalists about whether she'll tighten the lockdown screws even further, says "we have the harshest lockdown conditions that any state in Australia has seen".
"The vaccine is our key tool," she says, a line which jars with the Prime Minister's assertion that the lockdown itself is "the primary tool".
This week, the core message from senior NSW ministers and Chief Health Officer Dr Kerry Chant has been for the public to get behind a state-wide effort to achieve 6 million jabs (first or second doses) by the end of this month, which will equate to roughly half the state's population.
To reach the goal, every delivery channel available is being ramped up. Just under 4.3 million doses have been administered in NSW so far, of which about 2.7 million have been delivered via Commonwealth channels which include GPs, specialist respiratory clinics and aged and Indigenous healthcare.
The NSW government has been responsible for delivering the balance, about 1.6 million doses, through outlets which include three mass vaccination hubs at Sydney Olympic Park, Lake Macquarie, and Macquarie Fields in Sydney's south-west. Two more mass hubs are due to open in Wollongong and at the Qudos Bank Arena on Monday. To bolster the vaccine workforce, appropriately trained student dentists, podiatrists and speech pathologists among others are being brought in.
Local authorities are also pumping out vaccines via another 40 or so NSW-run clinics around the state, and through 100 "outreach" locations (where city-based medical teams fly to rural areas).
In the hotspots of south-west Sydney, pop-up and walk-in vaccination clinics targeting multicultural communities are now operating on set days a week at places such as mosques and community centres. A key walk-in clinic is operating seven days a week at Prairiewood.
The head of the federal government's Operation COVID Shield, Lieutenant General John Frewen, has proposed adding drive-in clinics to the mix, a move which caught the state government off-guard when it was announced mid-week.
But NSW Health Minister Brad Hazzard told the Herald he welcomed the initiative and "encouraged" Canberra to work with the state on determining the best locations, while "preferably getting on with it as fast as possible".
He's also welcomed federal help in getting vaccine programs into south-west Sydney's major distribution hubs for suppliers such as Woolworths, Coles, Aldi and Metcash.
So far, 21 per cent of the state's eligible residents have been fully vaccinated, including 45 per cent of over-70s. Between April and the end of last month, 2.53 million of those jabs were AstraZeneca and 1.51 million Pfizer. Most of the AstraZeneca went out through federally run channels. One in five over-70s have yet to receive a first dose, which Chant says is of grave concern.
For the state to reach its 6 million target by month's end, about 69,000 doses will have to be put into arms daily, a tall order given that weekends usually dip to around half the weekday rate. Yet it would seem within reach if NSW keeps up the pace of more than 80,000 doses administered every day this week.
On Wednesday, the figure exceeded 84,000 doses.
GPs are doing a substantial proportion of the heavy lifting: 1800 of the state's almost 3000 general practices are participating. Many small practices are turned off by the costs and staffing demands of the consent process, reporting requirements and a delivery protocol which requires observation of patients for 15 minutes after each jab.
Mount Druitt GP Kean-Seng Lim told the Herald: "GPs get a rebate $31 from Medicare for every first jab, then it drops to $24 for the second. It can be financially non-sustainable for many doctors."
The latest weapon to be added to the vaccine armoury is pharmacies, only now gearing up despite many having engaged with the federal government's "expression of interest" process in March.
Across the state, 1250 pharmacies have been authorised to join the program, though only about 300 have started inoculating customers
That number will climb swiftly next month as hundreds more join the rollout. NSW Pharmacy Guild president David Heffernan says "we've been ready to go from day one - it's just been the block of government".
Port Macquarie pharmacist Judy Plunkett says the vaccine rollout has been "singularly the most frustrating thing in [her life] for the past six months".
"If pharmacies were brought on in April, we could have done tens of thousands of doses by now," says Plunkett, who has given out 4000 flu vaccines this year.
Plunkett says many pharmacists are uniquely connected to their communities and are well-placed to help long-standing customers who might struggle with impenetrable booking systems and consent process.
She says: "The booking system is a mystery to most people. It's not accessible. It's not easy. And it's not selling itself. These are all people who often don't have cars and many have carers that bring them in to see me. They are saying 'Judy, we will wait for you to vaccinate us'."
At his family-owned Priceline pharmacy in Fairfield, pharmacist Quinn On has been staggered by demand for vaccine since he started administering AstraZeneca on July 26.
Having erected a gazebo on the pavement outside to keep patients socially distanced while he explained the vaccine process to them, Quinn discovered he and staff were attracting quite an audience among Cantonese, Mandarin, Vietnamese and Lao speakers (the pharmacy's staff are multilingual).
"We started noticing everyone was hanging around listening as we counselled people over the consent form and explained the risks and benefits of AstraZeneca. We managed to debunk a lot of the misinformation because many don't speak much English," Quinn says.
"We went from doing 25 on the first day to 172 on the third day."
He rapidly exhausted his government-allotted quota of 300 vaccine doses for the fortnight and had to borrow extra from a GP and another pharmacy. He's now won agreement to administer 600 a fortnight, but could do more.
Quinn says for some of his peers, the $19 paid per jab has been a deterrent, given the higher Medicare rebate paid to doctors. He finds the payment does not cover his costs. "But I say 'do it for the community - the more people we can do, the quicker we can get out of this'."
His experience highlights how effective local leaders can be in countering misinformation about the virus, which has seeped into Sydney's most vulnerable ethnic communities from overseas media and eroded confidence in vaccines.
Data from Operation COVID Shield revealed for the first time this week the glaring "vaccination gap" between some of Sydney's most privileged areas and the hardest-hit south-west. More than a quarter of those aged over 15 living in North Sydney, for instance, are fully vaccinated compared with just 14 per cent in the south-west.
As well as being the epicentre of the city's outbreak, the south-west is home to many of the key "authorised" workers who must leave the area daily to provide essential services such as aged care and food distribution.
Public health experts say a lack of easy access to vaccines, a constrained supply and complex booking systems have been to blame. Younger people - who predominate in the south-west - were locked out of AstraZeneca until very recently because of changing advice from the expert ATAGI advisory group. They've been denied Pfizer because of a lack of supply.
In the Hunter and Newcastle, now under tight lockdown for a week, there are also low rates of vaccination. In the Hunter, only 14 per cent are fully vaccinated.
On Thursday, Berejiklian announced a fast-tracked 180,000 Pfizer vaccines would be provided to NSW in the next fortnight, news which the Premier said had left her and her health officials "overjoyed".
Yet federal sources later confirmed these were the same jabs that had been foreshadowed by the Prime Minister on July 25.
The top-up comes after Canberra sent an extra 300,000 doses earlier in July - half Pfizer, half AstraZeneca - to boost jabs available for south-west Sydney. The Pfizer doses were used almost immediately on teachers and aged care workers, and state authorities asked the federal government to direct the AstraZeneca doses to community pharmacists instead of state hubs.
But last month, NSW administered 773,976 AstraZeneca doses compared to 325,086 in April. The message from local health authorities now is unambiguous: if you are 18 or over, don't wait - take whatever is available.
From September, the state should start to receive at least 290,000 Pfizer doses each week, together with 30,000 Moderna and at least 300,000 AstraZeneca.
Berejiklian has dropped veiled hints at minor loosening of the straitjacket once the state hits 6 million jabs. But the weight of epidemiological evidence leans heavily against that prospect.
Modelling carried out by the Doherty Institute for national cabinet makes clear vaccination rates have to reach about 70 per cent at least before any meaningful easing of restrictions can occur without a resurgence of the highly infectious Delta strain. An Infectious Diseases Society of America briefing this week painted a bleaker picture, saying Delta has pushed the threshold for herd immunity to well over 80 per cent and potentially 90 per cent.
Frewen has promised that every Australian who wants a vaccine will have the chance to get one by Christmas. By then, we may have exceeded the 70 per cent national vaccination uptake. It's anyone's guess what stomach the community will have for compliance with on-and-off lockdowns by then.
 
CREDIT: Deborah Snow and Lucy Carroll

Subject: Drug stores; COVID-19 vaccines; False information; Immunization; Coronaviruses; Pharmacists; Medicare; Consent

Don’t wait for Pfizer, get AZ now

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 05 Aug 2021: 1.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/don-t-wait-pfizer-get-az-now/docview/2557908100/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Don%E2%80%99t+wait+for+Pfizer%2C+get+AZ+now&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Don%26rsquo%3Bt+wait+for+Pfizer%2C+get+AZ+now&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=1&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: A group of the nation’s leading medical professionals has urged Australians not to wait for Pfizer and to get jabbed with Astra­Zeneca, as new Treasury modelling found that a lower vaccine take-up rate would wipe nearly $7bn off growth every three months.
The economic necessity of imposing short, sharp lockdowns and achieving higher inoculation rates was revealed in Treasury analysis that showed Australians would be forced into five lockdowns every quarter if vaccination coverage flatlined at 50 per cent.
In addition, Treasury found the cost of governments failing to act quickly to rein in outbreaks would rise to an even more ­brutal $35bn a quarter under the Delta strain.
The warning came as an alliance of prominent medical and health specialists penned an open letter debunking myths about the AstraZeneca vaccine and pleaded for all people to urgently get jabbed.
The letter, published in The Australian, reveals a growing number of doctors, professors and medical researchers are pushing back against the undermining of AstraZeneca, which they say provides similar protections against the Delta variant as Pfizer.
The public appeal includes doctors at the coal face of the Sydney outbreak and leading health professionals including: National Health and Medical Research Council chair Bruce Robinson; former deputy chief medical officer Nick Coatsworth; infectious diseases experts Tania Sorrell and Lyn Gilbert; Gardasil vaccine co-founder and former Australian of the Year Ian Frazer, and former Australian Medical Association president Steve Hambleton. The health professionals said the country was at a “critical point in the pandemic” and noted that, as NSW responded to spikes in cases and infections rose in Queensland, there were “millions of unused doses of AstraZeneca vaccine sitting in GPs’ surgeries and vaccination clinics”.
“Our strong recommendation to all Australians in an outbreak setting is to get a first dose of AstraZeneca or Pfizer vaccine as soon as possible ... This is consistent with the latest Australian Technical Advisory Group on Immunisation advice,” the health professionals wrote.
“Do not wait for the Pfizer ­vaccine if the AstraZeneca vaccine is available to you. AstraZeneca is an excellent vaccine that has ­already saved millions of lives and will save many more. Covid kills, and by the time you or someone you love is infected, hospitalised or in ICU it is too late.” They said lockdowns, while necessary to limit new cases, could not “prevent the spread of the virus” alone and caused “terrible stress and economic hardship”.
In a research paper assessing the direct economic costs of the COVID-19 management strategies modelled by the Doherty Institute, Treasury said it now assumed there would be five Covid-19 outbreaks every three months – two more than assumed in the May budget.
The lockdowns necessary to control the outbreaks would also be more prolonged. At a vaccination rate of 50 per cent, (the current rate is 20 per cent), there would be 70 days of lockdowns on average each quarter affecting 20 per cent of the population, while there would be lower-level health measures in place for the remaining 20 days.
Over the quarter, the average weekly cost of restrictions would be $570m – or $6.8bn in total – representing a 1.4 per cent handbrake on economic growth as long as vaccination rates remained relatively low.
Jarden chief economist Carlos Cacho said the official estimates showed vaccination was the economic imperative to next year’s recovery. Mr Cacho said that under this scenario the nation’s trend annual growth rate of roughly 3 per cent would halve. The combination of low immigration, which has accounted for about half of long-term growth, and low vaccination rates would mean the economy would stagnate.
Even at 60 per cent vaccination rates and with the adoption of a swift lockdown approach, Australia would still experience 48 days of lockdown on average in each quarter, Treasury estimated.
This would have an average weekly cost of $430m – equivalent to $5.2bn over three months, or closer to 1 per cent of nominal GDP. “Moderate or strict lockdowns are still expected to be necessary to continue minimising outbreaks until Australia reaches 70 per cent vaccination rates for Australian adults (aged 16 and over),” Treasury said. “As a result, the costs of managing COVID-19 will remain high.” By the time seven in 10 Australians aged 16 or over had received two jabs, Treasury assumed there would be only “periodic low-level restrictions” at an average cost of $200m a week or $2.4bn a quarter.
At an 80 per cent vaccination rate, the average weekly hit to economic growth would reduce to $140m or $1.7bn over a quarter.
While the costs of locking down Australians to control the spread of the virus are large, the economic damage from adopting a more laissez-faire approach to managing infections would be devastating. Assuming half the adult population was vaccinated and there was a failure to take swift action to lock down, the economic damage from more prolonged restrictions aimed at reining in infections would rise to as much as $35bn a quarter, according to the Treasury analysis.
Under this dire “managed transmission” scenario, authorities would only introduce lockdowns once the health system had reached its capacity to treat ­severely ill Covid patients. “Applying strict localised lockdowns, for shorter durations, is more cost-effective than applying more moderate lockdowns for longer periods,” Treasury concluded.
In their open letter, Australia’s leading health professionals outlined the low risks associated with AstraZeneca, upholding its safety and efficacy, while urging younger Australians to get vaccinated. “Whilst the risk of death is highest in older age groups, we are now seeing serious illness and death in unvaccinated young patients. The risk, however, is also not all about simple survival. Between 10-30 per cent of people who contract Covid will suffer long-term health consequences, the seriousness of which are yet to be fully understood,” they said.
CREDIT: GEOFF CHAMBERS; PATRICK COMMINS

Subject: Infections; COVID-19 vaccines; Costs; Economic growth; Coronaviruses; Immunization; Medical personnel; Epidemics; Medical research; Disease transmission

Business indexing term: Subject: Costs Economic growth
